<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chaplin_City_Lights_still.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="350" data-file-width="245" decoding="async" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Chaplin_City_Lights_still.jpg/170px-Chaplin_City_Lights_still.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/93/Chaplin_City_Lights_still.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chaplin_City_Lights_still.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/City_Lights" title="City Lights">City Lights</a></i> (1931) is regarded as one of Chaplin's finest works.</div></div></div>
<p>When filming began at the end of 1928, Chaplin had been working on the story for almost a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson398Maland200733–34,_41_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson398Maland200733–34,_41-212">[191]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/City_Lights" title="City Lights">City Lights</a></i> followed the Tramp's love for a blind flower girl (played by <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Cherrill" title="Virginia Cherrill">Virginia Cherrill</a>) and his efforts to raise money for her sight-saving operation. It was a challenging production that lasted 21 months,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson409_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson409-213">[192]</a></sup> with Chaplin later confessing that he "had worked himself into a neurotic state of wanting perfection".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaplin324_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaplin324-214">[193]</a></sup> One advantage Chaplin found in sound technology was the opportunity to record a musical score for the film, which he composed himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaplin324_214-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaplin324-214">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[194]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chaplin finished editing <i>City Lights</i> in December 1930, by which time silent films were an anachronism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson410_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson410-216">[195]</a></sup> A preview before an unsuspecting public audience was not a success,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaplin325_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaplin325-217">[196]</a></sup> but a showing for the press produced positive reviews. One journalist wrote, "Nobody in the world but Charlie Chaplin could have done it. He is the only person that has that peculiar something called 'audience appeal' in sufficient quality to defy the popular penchant for movies that talk."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson413_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson413-218">[197]</a></sup> Given its general release in January 1931, <i>City Lights</i> proved to be a popular and financial success, eventually grossing over $3<span class="nowrap"> </span>million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[v]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland2007108–110Chaplin328Robinson415_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland2007108–110Chaplin328Robinson415-220">[198]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/British_Film_Institute" title="British Film Institute">British Film Institute</a> cites it as Chaplin's finest accomplishment, and the critic <a href="/wiki/James_Agee" title="James Agee">James Agee</a> hails the closing scene as "the greatest piece of acting and the highest moment in movies".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bfi_great_features_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-bfi_great_features-221">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland200710–11_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland200710–11-222">[200]</a></sup> <i>City Lights</i> became Chaplin's personal favourite of his films and remained so throughout his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVance2003208_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVance2003208-223">[201]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Travels.2C_Paulette_Goddard.2C_and_Modern_Times"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Travels,_Paulette_Goddard,_and_Modern_Times">Travels, Paulette Goddard, and <i>Modern Times</i></span></h4>
<p><i>City Lights</i> had been a success, but Chaplin was unsure if he could make another picture without dialogue. He remained convinced that sound would not work in his films, but was also "obsessed by a depressing fear of being old-fashioned".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaplin360_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaplin360-224">[202]</a></sup> In this state of uncertainty, early in 1931, the comedian decided to take a holiday and ended up travelling for 16 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish243Robinson420_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish243Robinson420-225">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[w]</a></sup> He spent months travelling Western Europe, including extended stays in France and Switzerland, and spontaneously decided to visit Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson429–441_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson429–441-228">[205]</a></sup> The day after he arrived in Japan, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Inukai_Tsuyoshi" title="Inukai Tsuyoshi">Inukai Tsuyoshi</a> was assassinated by ultra-nationalists in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/May_15_Incident" title="May 15 Incident">May 15 Incident</a>. The group's original plan had been to provoke a war with the United States by assassinating Chaplin at a welcome reception organised by the prime minister, but the plan had been foiled due to delayed public announcement of the event's date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESilverberg1–2_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESilverberg1–2-229">[206]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Modern_Times_poster.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1120" data-file-width="736" decoding="async" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Modern_Times_poster.jpg/170px-Modern_Times_poster.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Modern_Times_poster.jpg/255px-Modern_Times_poster.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Modern_Times_poster.jpg/340px-Modern_Times_poster.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Modern_Times_poster.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Modern_Times_(film)" title="Modern Times (film)">Modern Times</a></i> (1936), described by Jérôme Larcher as a "grim contemplation on the automatization of the individual"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELarcher64_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELarcher64-230">[207]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In his autobiography, Chaplin recalled that on his return to Los Angeles, "I was confused and without plan, restless and conscious of an extreme loneliness". He briefly considered retiring and moving to China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaplin372,_375_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaplin372,_375-231">[208]</a></sup> Chaplin's loneliness was relieved when he met 21-year-old actress <a href="/wiki/Paulette_Goddard" title="Paulette Goddard">Paulette Goddard</a> in July 1932, and the pair began a relationship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson453Maland1989147_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson453Maland1989147-232">[209]</a></sup> He was not ready to commit to a film, however, and focused on writing a <a href="/wiki/Serial_(literature)" title="Serial (literature)">serial</a> about his travels (published in <i><a href="/wiki/Woman%27s_Home_Companion" title="Woman's Home Companion">Woman's Home Companion</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson451_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson451-233">[210]</a></sup> The trip had been a stimulating experience for Chaplin, including meetings with several prominent thinkers, and he became increasingly interested in world affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish256_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish256-234">[211]</a></sup> The state of labour in America troubled him, and he feared that capitalism and machinery in the workplace would increase unemployment levels. It was these concerns that stimulated Chaplin to develop his new film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELarcher63Robinson457–458_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELarcher63Robinson457–458-235">[212]</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Modern_Times_(film)" title="Modern Times (film)">Modern Times</a></i> was announced by Chaplin as "a satire on certain phases of our industrial life".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish257_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish257-236">[213]</a></sup> Featuring the Tramp and Goddard as they endure the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression_in_the_United_States" title="Great Depression in the United States">Great Depression</a>, it took ten and a half months to film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson465_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson465-237">[214]</a></sup> Chaplin intended to use spoken dialogue but changed his mind during rehearsals. Like its predecessor, <i>Modern Times</i> employed sound effects but almost no speaking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson466_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson466-238">[215]</a></sup> Chaplin's performance of a gibberish song did, however, give the Tramp a voice for the only time on film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson468_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson468-239">[216]</a></sup> After recording the music, Chaplin released <i>Modern Times</i> in February 1936.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson469–472,_474_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson469–472,_474-240">[217]</a></sup> It was his first feature in 15 years to adopt political references and social realism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989150_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989150-241">[218]</a></sup> a factor that attracted considerable press coverage despite Chaplin's attempts to downplay the issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989144–147_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989144–147-242">[219]</a></sup> The film earned less at the box-office than his previous features and received mixed reviews, as some viewers disliked the politicising.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989157Robinson473_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989157Robinson473-243">[220]</a></sup> Today, <i>Modern Times</i> is seen by the British Film Institute as one of Chaplin's "great features",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bfi_great_features_221-1"><a href="#cite_note-bfi_great_features-221">[199]</a></sup> while David Robinson says it shows the filmmaker at "his unrivalled peak as a creator of visual comedy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchneider125_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchneider125-244">[221]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the release of <i>Modern Times</i>, Chaplin left with Goddard for a trip to the Far East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson479_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson479-245">[222]</a></sup> The couple had refused to comment on the nature of their relationship, and it was not known whether they were married or not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson469_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson469-246">[223]</a></sup> Sometime later, Chaplin revealed that they married in <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Canton</a> during this trip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson483_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson483-247">[224]</a></sup> By 1938, the couple had drifted apart, as both focused heavily on their work, although Goddard was again his leading lady in his next feature film, <i>The Great Dictator</i>. She eventually divorced Chaplin in Mexico in 1942, citing incompatibility and separation for more than a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson509–510_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson509–510-248">[225]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1939.E2.80.931952:_controversies_and_fading_popularity"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1939–1952:_controversies_and_fading_popularity">1939–1952: controversies and fading popularity</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Great_Dictator"><i>The Great Dictator</i></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Great_Dictator_still_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="301" data-file-width="253" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/The_Great_Dictator_still_cropped.jpg/180px-The_Great_Dictator_still_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/35/The_Great_Dictator_still_cropped.jpg 1.5x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Great_Dictator_still_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chaplin satirised <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Dictator" title="The Great Dictator">The Great Dictator</a></i> (1940).</div></div></div>
<p>The 1940s saw Chaplin face a series of controversies, both in his work and in his personal life, which changed his fortunes and severely affected his popularity in the United States. The first of these was his growing boldness in expressing his political beliefs. Deeply disturbed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Para-fascism" title="Para-fascism">surge of militaristic nationalism</a> in 1930s world politics,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson485Maland1989159_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson485Maland1989159-249">[226]</a></sup> Chaplin found that he could not keep these issues out of his work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaplin386_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaplin386-250">[227]</a></sup> Parallels between himself and <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> had been widely noted: the pair were born four days apart, both had risen from poverty to world prominence, and Hitler wore <a href="/wiki/Toothbrush_moustache" title="Toothbrush moustache">the same moustache style</a> as Chaplin. It was this physical resemblance that supplied the plot for Chaplin's next film, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Dictator" title="The Great Dictator">The Great Dictator</a></i>, which directly satirised Hitler and attacked fascism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchickel28Maland1989165,_170Louvish271Robinson490Larcher67Kemp158_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchickel28Maland1989165,_170Louvish271Robinson490Larcher67Kemp158-251">[228]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chaplin spent two years developing the script<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaplin388_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaplin388-252">[229]</a></sup> and began filming in September 1939, six days after Britain declared war on Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson496_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson496-253">[230]</a></sup> He had submitted to using spoken dialogue, partly out of acceptance that he had no other choice, but also because he recognised it as a better method for delivering a political message.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989165_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989165-254">[231]</a></sup> Making a comedy about Hitler was seen as highly controversial, but Chaplin's financial independence allowed him to take the risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989164_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989164-255">[232]</a></sup> "I was determined to go ahead", he later wrote, "for Hitler must be laughed at."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaplin387_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaplin387-256">[233]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[x]</a></sup> Chaplin replaced the Tramp (while wearing similar attire) with "A Jewish Barber", a reference to the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">Nazi Party</a>'s belief that he was Jewish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[234]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[y]</a></sup> In a dual performance, he also played the dictator "Adenoid Hynkel", a parody of Hitler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989172–173_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989172–173-261">[236]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>The Great Dictator</i> spent a year in production and was released in October 1940.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson505,_507_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson505,_507-262">[237]</a></sup> The film generated a vast amount of publicity, with a critic for <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> calling it "the most eagerly awaited picture of the year", and it was one of the biggest money-makers of the era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989169,_178–179_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989169,_178–179-263">[238]</a></sup> The ending was unpopular, however, and generated controversy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989176Schickel30–31_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989176Schickel30–31-264">[239]</a></sup> Chaplin concluded the film with a five-minute speech in which he abandoned his barber character, looked directly into the camera, and pleaded against war and fascism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989179–181Louvish282Robinson504_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989179–181Louvish282Robinson504-265">[240]</a></sup> Charles J. Maland has identified this overt preaching as triggering a decline in Chaplin's popularity, and writes, "Henceforth, no movie fan would ever be able to separate the dimension of politics from [his] star image".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989178–179_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989178–179-266">[241]</a></sup> Nevertheless, both <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> liked the film, which they saw at private screenings before its release. Roosevelt subsequently invited Chaplin to read the film's final speech over the radio during his January 1941 inauguration, with the speech becoming a "hit" of the celebration. Chaplin was often invited to other patriotic functions to read the speech to audiences during the years of the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGehring133_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGehring133-267">[242]</a></sup> <i>The Great Dictator</i> received five Academy Award nominations, including <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Picture" title="Academy Award for Best Picture">Best Picture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Original_Screenplay" title="Academy Award for Best Original Screenplay">Best Original Screenplay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Actor" title="Academy Award for Best Actor">Best Actor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[243]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Legal_troubles_and_Oona_O.27Neill"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_troubles_and_Oona_O'Neill">Legal troubles and Oona O'Neill</span></h4>
<p>In the mid-1940s, Chaplin was involved in a series of trials that occupied most of his time and significantly affected his public image.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989197–198_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989197–198-269">[244]</a></sup> The troubles stemmed from his affair with an aspiring actress named <a href="/wiki/Joan_Barry_(American_actress)" title="Joan Barry (American actress)">Joan Barry</a>, with whom he was involved intermittently between June 1941 and the autumn of 1942.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989200_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989200-270">[245]</a></sup> Barry, who displayed obsessive behaviour and was twice arrested after they separated,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[z]</a></sup> reappeared the following year and announced that she was pregnant with Chaplin's child. As Chaplin denied the claim, Barry filed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paternity_suit" title="Paternity suit">paternity suit</a> against him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989198–201_271-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989198–201-271">[246]</a></sup>
</p><p>The director of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">Federal Bureau of Investigation</a> (FBI), <a href="/wiki/J._Edgar_Hoover" title="J. Edgar Hoover">J. Edgar Hoover</a>, who had long been suspicious of Chaplin's political leanings, used the opportunity to generate negative publicity about him. As part of a <a href="/wiki/Smear_campaign" title="Smear campaign">smear campaign</a> to damage Chaplin's image,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENowell-Smith85_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENowell-Smith85-273">[247]</a></sup> the FBI named him in four indictments related to the Barry case. Most serious of these was an alleged violation of the <a href="/wiki/Mann_Act" title="Mann Act">Mann Act</a>, which prohibits the transportation of women across state boundaries for sexual purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[aa]</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Otto_Friedrich" title="Otto Friedrich">Otto Friedrich</a> called this an "absurd prosecution" of an "ancient statute",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriedrich190,_393_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriedrich190,_393-277">[250]</a></sup> yet if Chaplin was found guilty, he faced 23 years in jail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989215_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989215-278">[251]</a></sup> Three charges lacked sufficient evidence to proceed to court, but the Mann Act trial began on 21 March 1944.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[252]</a></sup> Chaplin was acquitted two weeks later, on<span class="nowrap"> </span>4 April.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[253]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989204–205_274-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989204–205-274">[248]</a></sup> The case was frequently headline news, with <i><a href="/wiki/Newsweek" title="Newsweek">Newsweek</a></i> calling it the "biggest public relations scandal since the <a href="/wiki/Roscoe_Arbuckle" title="Roscoe Arbuckle">Fatty Arbuckle</a> murder trial in 1921".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989214–215_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989214–215-281">[254]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oona_O%27Neill_-_1943.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="864" data-file-width="660" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Oona_O%27Neill_-_1943.jpg/170px-Oona_O%27Neill_-_1943.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Oona_O%27Neill_-_1943.jpg/255px-Oona_O%27Neill_-_1943.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Oona_O%27Neill_-_1943.jpg/340px-Oona_O%27Neill_-_1943.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oona_O%27Neill_-_1943.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chaplin's fourth wife and widow, <a href="/wiki/Oona_O%27Neill" title="Oona O'Neill">Oona</a></div></div></div>
<p>Barry's child, Carol Ann, was born in October 1943, and the paternity suit went to court in December 1944. After two arduous trials, in which the prosecuting lawyer accused him of "<a href="/wiki/Moral_turpitude" title="Moral turpitude">moral turpitude</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvishxiii_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvishxiii-282">[255]</a></sup> Chaplin was declared to be the father. Evidence from blood tests that indicated otherwise were not admissible,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[ab]</a></sup> and the judge ordered Chaplin to pay child support until Carol Ann turned 21. Media coverage of the suit was influenced by the FBI, which fed information to gossip columnist <a href="/wiki/Hedda_Hopper" title="Hedda Hopper">Hedda Hopper</a>, and Chaplin was portrayed in an overwhelmingly critical light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrost74–88Maland1989207–213Sbardellati_and_Shaw508Friedrich393_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrost74–88Maland1989207–213Sbardellati_and_Shaw508Friedrich393-285">[257]</a></sup>
</p><p>The controversy surrounding Chaplin increased when – two weeks after the paternity suit was filed – it was announced that he had married his newest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prot%C3%A9g%C3%A9e" title="Protégée">protégée</a>, 18-year-old <a href="/wiki/Oona_O%27Neill" title="Oona O'Neill">Oona O'Neill</a>, the daughter of American playwright <a href="/wiki/Eugene_O%27Neill" title="Eugene O'Neill">Eugene O'Neill</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish135_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish135-286">[258]</a></sup> Chaplin, then 54, had been introduced to her by a film agent seven months earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[ac]</a></sup> In his autobiography, Chaplin described meeting O'Neill as "the happiest event of my life", and claimed to have found "perfect love".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaplin423,_477_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaplin423,_477-290">[261]</a></sup> Chaplin's son, Charles III, reported that Oona "worshipped" his father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson519_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson519-291">[262]</a></sup> The couple remained married until Chaplin's death, and had eight children over 18 years: <a href="/wiki/Geraldine_Chaplin" title="Geraldine Chaplin">Geraldine Leigh</a> (b. July 1944), <a href="/wiki/Michael_Chaplin_(actor)" title="Michael Chaplin (actor)">Michael John</a> (b. March 1946), <a href="/wiki/Josephine_Chaplin" title="Josephine Chaplin">Josephine Hannah</a> (b. March 1949), <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Chaplin" title="Victoria Chaplin">Victoria Agnes</a> (b. May 1951), <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Chaplin" title="Eugene Chaplin">Eugene Anthony</a> (b. August 1953), Jane Cecil (b. May 1957), Annette Emily (b. December 1959), and <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Chaplin" title="Christopher Chaplin">Christopher James</a> (b. July 1962).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson671–675_292-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson671–675-292">[263]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Monsieur_Verdoux_and_communist_accusations"><i>Monsieur Verdoux</i> and communist accusations</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Monsieur_Verdoux_poster.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1419" data-file-width="936" decoding="async" height="258" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Monsieur_Verdoux_poster.jpg/170px-Monsieur_Verdoux_poster.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Monsieur_Verdoux_poster.jpg/255px-Monsieur_Verdoux_poster.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Monsieur_Verdoux_poster.jpg/340px-Monsieur_Verdoux_poster.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Monsieur_Verdoux_poster.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Monsieur_Verdoux" title="Monsieur Verdoux">Monsieur Verdoux</a></i> (1947), a dark comedy about a serial killer, marked a significant departure for Chaplin.</div></div></div>
<p>Chaplin claimed that the Barry trials had "crippled [his] creativeness", and it was some time before he began working again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaplin426_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaplin426-293">[264]</a></sup> In April 1946, he finally began filming a project that had been in development since 1942.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson520_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson520-294">[265]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Monsieur_Verdoux" title="Monsieur Verdoux">Monsieur Verdoux</a></i> was a <a href="/wiki/Black_comedy" title="Black comedy">black comedy</a>, the story of a French bank clerk, Verdoux (Chaplin), who loses his job and begins marrying and murdering wealthy widows to support his family. Chaplin's inspiration for the project came from <a href="/wiki/Orson_Welles" title="Orson Welles">Orson Welles</a>, who wanted him to star in a film about the French <a href="/wiki/Serial_killer" title="Serial killer">serial killer</a> <a href="/wiki/Henri_D%C3%A9sir%C3%A9_Landru" title="Henri Désiré Landru">Henri Désiré Landru</a>. Chaplin decided that the concept would "make a wonderful comedy",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaplin412_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaplin412-295">[266]</a></sup> and paid Welles $5,000<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[ad]</a></sup> for the idea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson519–520_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson519–520-297">[267]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chaplin again vocalised his political views in <i>Monsieur Verdoux</i>, criticising <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a> and arguing that the world encourages mass killing through wars and <a href="/wiki/Weapon_of_mass_destruction" title="Weapon of mass destruction">weapons of mass destruction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish304Sbardellati_and_Shaw501_298-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish304Sbardellati_and_Shaw501-298">[268]</a></sup> Because of this, the film met with controversy when it was released in April 1947;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish296–297Robinson538–543Larcher77_299-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish296–297Robinson538–543Larcher77-299">[269]</a></sup> Chaplin was booed at the premiere, and there were calls for a boycott.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish296–297Sbardellati_and_Shaw503_300-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish296–297Sbardellati_and_Shaw503-300">[270]</a></sup> <i>Monsieur Verdoux</i> was the first Chaplin release that failed both critically and commercially in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989235–245,_250_301-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989235–245,_250-301">[271]</a></sup> It was more successful abroad,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989250_302-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989250-302">[272]</a></sup> and Chaplin's screenplay was nominated at the <a href="/wiki/20th_Academy_Awards" title="20th Academy Awards">Academy Awards</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish297_303-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish297-303">[273]</a></sup> He was proud of the film, writing in his autobiography, "<i>Monsieur Verdoux</i> is the cleverest and most brilliant film I have yet made."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaplin444_304-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaplin444-304">[274]</a></sup>
</p><p>The negative reaction to <i>Monsieur Verdoux</i> was largely the result of changes in Chaplin's public image.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989251_305-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989251-305">[275]</a></sup> Along with damage of the Joan Barry scandal, he was publicly accused of being a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communist" title="Communist">communist</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson538–539Friedrich287_306-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson538–539Friedrich287-306">[276]</a></sup> His political activity had heightened during World War II, when he campaigned for the opening of a Second Front to help the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and supported various Soviet–American friendship groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989253_307-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989253-307">[277]</a></sup> He was also friendly with several suspected communists, and attended functions given by Soviet diplomats in Los Angeles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989221–226,_253–254_308-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989221–226,_253–254-308">[278]</a></sup> In the political climate of 1940s America, such activities meant Chaplin was considered, as Larcher writes, "dangerously <a href="/wiki/Progressivism" title="Progressivism">progressive</a> and amoral".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELarcher75Sbardellati_and_Shaw506Louvishxiii_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELarcher75Sbardellati_and_Shaw506Louvishxiii-309">[279]</a></sup> The FBI wanted him out of the country,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESbardellati152_310-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESbardellati152-310">[280]</a></sup> and launched an official investigation in early 1947.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989265–266_311-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989265–266-311">[281]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[ae]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chaplin denied being a communist, instead calling himself a "peacemonger",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvishxiv,_310Chaplin458Maland1989238_314-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvishxiv,_310Chaplin458Maland1989238-314">[283]</a></sup> but felt the government's effort to suppress the ideology was an unacceptable infringement of <a href="/wiki/Civil_liberties" title="Civil liberties">civil liberties</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson544_315-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson544-315">[284]</a></sup> Unwilling to be quiet about the issue, he openly protested against the trials of <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_USA" title="Communist Party USA">Communist Party</a> members and the activities of the <a href="/wiki/House_Un-American_Activities_Committee" title="House Un-American Activities Committee">House Un-American Activities Committee</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989255–256_316-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989255–256-316">[285]</a></sup> Chaplin received a <a href="/wiki/Subpoena" title="Subpoena">subpoena</a> to appear before HUAC but was not called to testify.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriedrich286Maland1989261_317-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriedrich286Maland1989261-317">[286]</a></sup> As his activities were widely reported in the press, and <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> fears grew, questions were raised over his failure to take American citizenship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELarcher80Sbardellati_and_Shaw510LouvishxiiiRobinson545_318-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELarcher80Sbardellati_and_Shaw510LouvishxiiiRobinson545-318">[287]</a></sup> Calls were made for him to be deported; in one extreme and widely published example, Representative <a href="/wiki/John_E._Rankin" title="John E. Rankin">John E. Rankin</a>, who helped establish HUAC, told <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a> in June 1947: "[Chaplin's] very life in Hollywood is detrimental to the moral fabric of America. [If he is deported]<span class="nowrap"> </span>... his loathsome pictures can be kept from before the eyes of the American youth. He should be deported and gotten rid of at once."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson545_319-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson545-319">[288]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2003, declassified British archives belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Foreign,_Commonwealth_and_Development_Office" title="Foreign, Commonwealth and Development Office">British Foreign Office</a> revealed that <a href="/wiki/George_Orwell" title="George Orwell">George Orwell</a> secretly accused Chaplin of being a secret communist and a friend of the USSR.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_320-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-320">[289]</a></sup> Chaplin's name was one of 35 Orwell gave to the <a href="/wiki/Information_Research_Department" title="Information Research Department">Information Research Department (IRD)</a>, a secret British Cold War propaganda department which worked closely with the CIA, according to a 1949 document known as <a href="/wiki/Orwell%27s_list" title="Orwell's list">Orwell's list</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_320-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-320">[289]</a></sup> Chaplin was not the only actor in America Orwell accused of being a secret communist. He also described American civil-rights leader and actor <a href="/wiki/Paul_Robeson" title="Paul Robeson">Paul Robeson</a> as being "anti-white".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_320-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-320">[289]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Limelight_and_banning_from_the_United_States"><i>Limelight</i> and banning from the United States</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Limelight_promo_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="378" data-file-width="425" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Limelight_promo_crop.jpg/220px-Limelight_promo_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Limelight_promo_crop.jpg/330px-Limelight_promo_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5e/Limelight_promo_crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Limelight_promo_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Limelight_(1952_film)" title="Limelight (1952 film)">Limelight</a></i> (1952) was a serious and autobiographical film for Chaplin. His character, Calvero, is an ex–<a href="/wiki/Music_hall" title="Music hall">music hall</a> star (described in this image as a "Tramp Comedian") forced to deal with his loss of popularity.</div></div></div>
<p>Although Chaplin remained politically active in the years following the failure of <i>Monsieur Verdoux</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[af]</a></sup> his next film, about a forgotten music hall comedian and a young ballerina in <a href="/wiki/Edwardian_era" title="Edwardian era">Edwardian</a> London, was devoid of political themes. <i><a href="/wiki/Limelight_(1952_film)" title="Limelight (1952 film)">Limelight</a></i> was heavily autobiographical, alluding not only to Chaplin's childhood and the lives of his parents, but also to his loss of popularity in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989288–290Robinson551–552Louvish312_323-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989288–290Robinson551–552Louvish312-323">[291]</a></sup> The cast included various members of his family, including his five oldest children and his half-brother, Wheeler Dryden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989293_324-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989293-324">[292]</a></sup>
</p><p>Filming began in November 1951, by which time Chaplin had spent three years working on the story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish317_325-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish317-325">[293]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[ag]</a></sup> He aimed for a more serious tone than any of his previous films, regularly using the word "melancholy" when explaining his plans to his co-star <a href="/wiki/Claire_Bloom" title="Claire Bloom">Claire Bloom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson562_328-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson562-328">[295]</a></sup> <i>Limelight</i> featured a cameo appearance from <a href="/wiki/Buster_Keaton" title="Buster Keaton">Buster Keaton</a>, whom Chaplin cast as his stage partner in a pantomime scene. This marked the only time the comedians worked together in a feature film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson567–568_329-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson567–568-329">[296]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chaplin decided to hold the world premiere of <i>Limelight</i> in London, since it was the setting of the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish326_330-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish326-330">[297]</a></sup> As he left Los Angeles, he expressed a premonition that he would not be returning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson570_331-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson570-331">[298]</a></sup> At New York, he boarded the <a href="/wiki/RMS_Queen_Elizabeth" title="RMS Queen Elizabeth">RMS <i>Queen Elizabeth</i></a> with his family on 18 September 1952.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989280_332-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989280-332">[299]</a></sup> The next day, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Attorney_General" title="United States Attorney General">United States Attorney General</a> <a href="/wiki/James_P._McGranery" title="James P. McGranery">James P. McGranery</a> revoked Chaplin's <a href="/wiki/Re-entry_permit" title="Re-entry permit">re-entry permit</a> and stated that he would have to submit to an interview concerning his political views and moral behaviour to re-enter the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989280_332-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989280-332">[299]</a></sup> Although McGranery told the press that he had "a pretty good case against Chaplin", Maland has concluded, on the basis of the FBI files that were released in the 1980s, that the US government had no real evidence to prevent Chaplin's re-entry. It is likely that he would have gained entry if he had applied for it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989280–287Sbardellati_and_Shaw520–521_333-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989280–287Sbardellati_and_Shaw520–521-333">[300]</a></sup> However, when Chaplin received a cablegram informing him of the news, he privately decided to cut his ties with the United States:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Whether I re-entered that unhappy country or not was of little consequence to me. I would like to have told them that the sooner I was rid of that hate-beleaguered atmosphere the better, that I was fed up of America's insults and moral pomposity<span class="nowrap"> </span>...<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaplin455_334-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaplin455-334">[301]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Because all of his property remained in America, Chaplin refrained from saying anything negative about the incident to the press.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson573_335-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson573-335">[302]</a></sup> The scandal attracted vast attention,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish330_336-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish330-336">[303]</a></sup> but Chaplin and his film were warmly received in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989280_332-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989280-332">[299]</a></sup> In America, the hostility towards him continued, and, although it received some positive reviews, <i>Limelight</i> was subjected to a wide-scale boycott.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989295–298,_307–311_337-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989295–298,_307–311-337">[304]</a></sup> Reflecting on this, Maland writes that Chaplin's fall, from an "unprecedented" level of popularity, "may be the most dramatic in the history of stardom in America".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989189_338-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989189-338">[305]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1953.E2.80.931977:_European_years"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1953–1977:_European_years">1953–1977: European years</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Move_to_Switzerland_and_A_King_in_New_York">Move to Switzerland and <i>A King in New York</i></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1023981488" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote rquote" style="float: right; width: 33%;"><p>I have been the object of lies and propaganda by powerful reactionary groups who, by their influence and by the aid of America's yellow press, have created an unhealthy atmosphere in which liberal-minded individuals can be singled out and persecuted. Under these conditions I find it virtually impossible to continue my motion-picture work, and I have therefore given up my residence in the United States.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Charlie Chaplin's press release regarding his decision not to seek re‑entry to the US<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELarcher89_339-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELarcher89-339">[306]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Chaplin did not attempt to return to the United States after his re-entry permit was revoked, and instead sent his wife to settle his affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-341"><a href="#cite_note-341">[ah]</a></sup> The couple decided to settle in Switzerland and, in January 1953, the family moved into their permanent home: <a href="/wiki/Manoir_de_Ban" title="Manoir de Ban">Manoir de Ban</a>, a 14-hectare (35-acre) estate<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-342"><a href="#cite_note-342">[308]</a></sup> overlooking <a href="/wiki/Lake_Geneva" title="Lake Geneva">Lake Geneva</a> in <a href="/wiki/Corsier-sur-Vevey" title="Corsier-sur-Vevey">Corsier-sur-Vevey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson580–581_343-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson580–581-343">[309]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345">[ai]</a></sup> Chaplin put his Beverly Hills house and studio up for sale in March, and surrendered his re-entry permit in April. The next year, his wife renounced her US citizenship and became a British citizen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson584,_674_346-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson584,_674-346">[311]</a></sup> Chaplin severed the last of his professional ties with the United States in 1955, when he sold the remainder of his stock in United Artists, which had been in financial difficulty since the early 1940s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynn466–467Robinson584Balio17–21_347-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynn466–467Robinson584Balio17–21-347">[312]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chaplin remained a controversial figure throughout the 1950s, especially after he was awarded the <a href="/wiki/World_Peace_Council_prizes" title="World Peace Council prizes">International Peace Prize</a> by the communist-led <a href="/wiki/World_Peace_Council" title="World Peace Council">World Peace Council</a>, and after his meetings with <a href="/wiki/Zhou_Enlai" title="Zhou Enlai">Zhou Enlai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989318Robinson584_348-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989318Robinson584-348">[313]</a></sup> He began developing his first European film, <i><a href="/wiki/A_King_in_New_York" title="A King in New York">A King in New York</a></i>, in 1954.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson585_349-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson585-349">[314]</a></sup> Casting himself as an exiled king who seeks asylum in the United States, Chaplin included several of his recent experiences in the screenplay. His son, Michael, was cast as a boy whose parents are targeted by the FBI, while Chaplin's character faces accusations of communism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvishxiv–xv_350-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvishxiv–xv-350">[315]</a></sup> The political satire parodied HUAC and attacked elements of 1950s culture – including consumerism, plastic surgery, and wide-screen cinema.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish341Maland1989320–321Robinson588–589Larcher89–90_351-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish341Maland1989320–321Robinson588–589Larcher89–90-351">[316]</a></sup> In a review, the playwright <a href="/wiki/John_Osborne" title="John Osborne">John Osborne</a> called it Chaplin's "most bitter" and "most openly personal" film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson587–589_352-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson587–589-352">[317]</a></sup> In a 1957 interview, when asked to clarify his political views, Chaplin stated "As for politics, I am an anarchist. I hate government and rules – and fetters<span class="nowrap"> </span>... People must be free."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-353"><a href="#cite_note-353">[318]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chaplin founded a new production company, Attica, and used <a href="/wiki/Shepperton_Studios" title="Shepperton Studios">Shepperton Studios</a> for the shooting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson585_349-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson585-349">[314]</a></sup> Filming in England proved a difficult experience, as he was used to his own Hollywood studio and familiar crew, and no longer had limitless production time. According to Robinson, this had an effect on the quality of the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEpstein137Robinson587_354-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEpstein137Robinson587-354">[319]</a></sup> <i>A King in New York</i> was released in September 1957, and received mixed reviews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynn506Louvish342Maland1989322_355-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynn506Louvish342Maland1989322-355">[320]</a></sup> Chaplin banned American journalists from its Paris première and decided not to release the film in the United States. This severely limited its revenue, although it achieved moderate commercial success in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson591_356-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson591-356">[321]</a></sup> <i>A King in New York</i> was not shown in America until 1973.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish347_357-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish347-357">[322]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVance2003329_358-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVance2003329-358">[323]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_works_and_renewed_appreciation">Final works and renewed appreciation</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chaplin_family_1961.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="517" data-file-width="928" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Chaplin_family_1961.jpg/280px-Chaplin_family_1961.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Chaplin_family_1961.jpg/420px-Chaplin_family_1961.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Chaplin_family_1961.jpg/560px-Chaplin_family_1961.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chaplin_family_1961.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chaplin with his wife Oona and six of their children in 1961</div></div></div>
<p>In the last two decades of his career, Chaplin concentrated on re-editing and scoring his old films for re-release, along with securing their ownership and distribution rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989326_359-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989326-359">[324]</a></sup> In an interview he granted in 1959, the year of his 70th birthday, Chaplin stated that there was still "room for the Little Man in the atomic age".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson594–595_360-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson594–595-360">[325]</a></sup> The first of these re-releases was <i><a href="/wiki/The_Chaplin_Revue" title="The Chaplin Revue">The Chaplin Revue</a></i> (1959), which included new versions of <i>A Dog's Life</i>, <i>Shoulder Arms</i>, and <i>The Pilgrim</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson594–595_360-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson594–595-360">[325]</a></sup>
</p><p>In America, the political atmosphere began to change and attention was once again directed to Chaplin's films instead of his views.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989326_359-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989326-359">[324]</a></sup> In July 1962, <i>The New York Times</i> published an editorial stating that "we do not believe the Republic would be in danger if yesterday's unforgotten little tramp were allowed to amble down the gangplank of a steamer or plane in an American port".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynn507–508_361-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynn507–508-361">[326]</a></sup> The same month, Chaplin was invested with the honorary degree of <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Letters" title="Doctor of Letters">Doctor of Letters</a> by the universities of <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">Oxford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Durham_University" title="Durham University">Durham</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson598–599_362-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson598–599-362">[327]</a></sup> In November 1963, the Plaza Theater in New York started a year-long series of Chaplin's films, including <i>Monsieur Verdoux</i> and <i>Limelight</i>, which gained excellent reviews from American critics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynn509Maland1989330_363-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynn509Maland1989330-363">[328]</a></sup> September 1964 saw the release of Chaplin's memoirs, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/My_Autobiography_(Chaplin)" title="My Autobiography (Chaplin)">My Autobiography</a></i>, which he had been working on since 1957.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson602–605_364-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson602–605-364">[329]</a></sup> The 500-page book became a worldwide best-seller. It focused on his early years and personal life, and was criticised for lacking information on his film career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson605–607Lynn510–512_365-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson605–607Lynn510–512-365">[330]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shortly after the publication of his memoirs, Chaplin began work on <i><a href="/wiki/A_Countess_from_Hong_Kong" title="A Countess from Hong Kong">A Countess from Hong Kong</a></i> (1967), a romantic comedy based on a script he had written for Paulette Goddard in the 1930s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson608–609_366-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson608–609-366">[331]</a></sup> Set on an ocean liner, it starred <a href="/wiki/Marlon_Brando" title="Marlon Brando">Marlon Brando</a> as an American ambassador and <a href="/wiki/Sophia_Loren" title="Sophia Loren">Sophia Loren</a> as a stowaway found in his cabin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson608–609_366-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson608–609-366">[331]</a></sup> The film differed from Chaplin's earlier productions in several aspects. It was his first to use <a href="/wiki/Technicolor" title="Technicolor">Technicolor</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Widescreen" title="Widescreen">widescreen</a> format, while he concentrated on directing and appeared on-screen only in a cameo role as a seasick steward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson612_367-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson612-367">[332]</a></sup> He also signed a deal with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_Studios" title="Universal Studios">Universal Pictures</a> and appointed his assistant, Jerome Epstein, as the producer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson607_368-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson607-368">[333]</a></sup> Chaplin was paid $600,000 director's fee as well as a percentage of the gross receipts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVance2003330_369-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVance2003330-369">[334]</a></sup> <i>A Countess from Hong Kong</i> premiered in January 1967, to unfavourable reviews, and was a box-office failure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEpstein192–196_370-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEpstein192–196-370">[335]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynn518Maland1989335_371-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynn518Maland1989335-371">[336]</a></sup> Chaplin was deeply hurt by the negative reaction to the film, which turned out to be his last.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEpstein192–196_370-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEpstein192–196-370">[335]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chaplin had a series of minor strokes in the late 1960s, which marked the beginning of a slow decline in his health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson619_372-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson619-372">[337]</a></sup> Despite the setbacks, he was soon writing a new film script, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Freak" title="The Freak">The Freak</a></i>, a story of a winged girl found in South America, which he intended as a starring vehicle for his daughter, Victoria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson619_372-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson619-372">[337]</a></sup> His fragile health prevented the project from being realised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEpstein203_373-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEpstein203-373">[338]</a></sup> In the early 1970s, Chaplin concentrated on re-releasing his old films, including <i>The Kid</i> and <i>The Circus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson620–621_374-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson620–621-374">[339]</a></sup> In 1971, he was made a <a href="/wiki/Legion_of_Honour" title="Legion of Honour">Commander of the National Order of the Legion of Honour</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Cannes_Film_Festival" title="Cannes Film Festival">Cannes Film Festival</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson621_375-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson621-375">[340]</a></sup> The following year, he was honoured with a special award by the <a href="/wiki/Venice_Film_Festival" title="Venice Film Festival">Venice Film Festival</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson625_376-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson625-376">[341]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chaplin_oscar.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="743" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Chaplin_oscar.JPG/220px-Chaplin_oscar.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Chaplin_oscar.JPG/330px-Chaplin_oscar.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Chaplin_oscar.JPG/440px-Chaplin_oscar.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chaplin_oscar.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chaplin (right) receiving his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honorary_Academy_Award" title="Honorary Academy Award">Honorary Academy Award</a> from <a href="/wiki/Jack_Lemmon" title="Jack Lemmon">Jack Lemmon</a> in 1972. It was the first time he had been to the United States in twenty years.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1972, the <a href="/wiki/Academy_of_Motion_Picture_Arts_and_Sciences" title="Academy of Motion Picture Arts and Sciences">Academy of Motion Picture Arts and Sciences</a> offered Chaplin an Honorary Award, which Robinson sees as a sign that America "wanted to make amends". Chaplin was initially hesitant about accepting but decided to return to the US for the first time in 20 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson621_375-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson621-375">[340]</a></sup> The visit attracted a large amount of press coverage and, at the Academy Awards gala, he was given a 12-minute standing ovation, the longest in the academy's history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-377"><a href="#cite_note-377">[342]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989347_378-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989347-378">[343]</a></sup> Visibly emotional, Chaplin accepted his award for "the incalculable effect he has had in making motion pictures the art form of this century".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson623–625_379-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson623–625-379">[344]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although Chaplin still had plans for future film projects, by the mid-1970s he was very frail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson627–628_380-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson627–628-380">[345]</a></sup> He experienced several further strokes, which made it difficult for him to communicate, and he had to use a wheelchair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson626_381-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson626-381">[346]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EugeneChaplin_382-0"><a href="#cite_note-EugeneChaplin-382">[347]</a></sup> His final projects were compiling a pictorial autobiography, <i>My Life in Pictures</i> (1974) and scoring <i>A Woman of Paris</i> for re-release in 1976.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson626–628_383-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson626–628-383">[348]</a></sup> He also appeared in a documentary about his life, <i>The Gentleman Tramp</i> (1975), directed by Richard Patterson.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynn534–536_384-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynn534–536-384">[349]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/1975_New_Year_Honours" title="1975 New Year Honours">1975 New Year Honours</a>, Chaplin was awarded a knighthood by Queen <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth II</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson626–628_383-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson626–628-383">[348]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-386"><a href="#cite_note-386">[aj]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-387"><a href="#cite_note-387">[351]</a></sup> though he was too weak to kneel and received the honour in his wheelchair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-388"><a href="#cite_note-388">[352]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Chaplin_Grave_in_Corsier-sur-Vevey.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="370" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Charles_Chaplin_Grave_in_Corsier-sur-Vevey.jpg/170px-Charles_Chaplin_Grave_in_Corsier-sur-Vevey.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Charles_Chaplin_Grave_in_Corsier-sur-Vevey.jpg/255px-Charles_Chaplin_Grave_in_Corsier-sur-Vevey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Charles_Chaplin_Grave_in_Corsier-sur-Vevey.jpg/340px-Charles_Chaplin_Grave_in_Corsier-sur-Vevey.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Chaplin_Grave_in_Corsier-sur-Vevey.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chaplin's grave in Corsier-sur-Vevey, Switzerland</div></div></div>
<p>By October 1977, Chaplin's health had declined to the point that he needed constant care.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson629_389-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson629-389">[353]</a></sup> In the early morning of Christmas Day 1977, Chaplin died at home after having a stroke in his sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EugeneChaplin_382-1"><a href="#cite_note-EugeneChaplin-382">[347]</a></sup> He was 88 years old. The funeral, on 27 December, was a small and private <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglican</a> ceremony, according to his wishes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVance2003359_390-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVance2003359-390">[354]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-392"><a href="#cite_note-392">[ak]</a></sup> Chaplin was interred in the Corsier-sur-Vevey cemetery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson629_389-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson629-389">[353]</a></sup> Among the film industry's tributes, director <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Clair" title="René Clair">René Clair</a> wrote, "He was a monument of the cinema, of all countries and all times<span class="nowrap"> </span>... the most beautiful gift the cinema made to us."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson631_393-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson631-393">[356]</a></sup> Actor <a href="/wiki/Bob_Hope" title="Bob Hope">Bob Hope</a> declared, "We were lucky to have lived in his time."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson632_394-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson632-394">[357]</a></sup> Chaplin left more than $100 million to his widow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hattenstone20210621_395-0"><a href="#cite_note-hattenstone20210621-395">[358]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 1 March 1978, Chaplin's coffin was dug up and stolen from its grave by Roman Wardas and Gantcho Ganev. The body was held for <a href="/wiki/Ransom" title="Ransom">ransom</a> in an attempt to extort money from his widow, Oona Chaplin. The pair were caught in a large police operation in May, and Chaplin's coffin was found buried in a field in the nearby village of <a href="/wiki/Noville,_Switzerland" title="Noville, Switzerland">Noville</a>. It was re-interred in the Corsier cemetery in a reinforced concrete vault.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-396"><a href="#cite_note-396">[359]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson629–631_397-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson629–631-397">[360]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Filmmaking">Filmmaking</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influences">Influences</span></h3>
<p>Chaplin believed his first influence to be his mother, who entertained him as a child by sitting at the window and mimicking passers-by: "it was through watching her that I learned not only how to express emotions with my hands and face, but also how to observe and study people."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson18_398-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson18-398">[361]</a></sup> Chaplin's early years in music hall allowed him to see stage comedians at work; he also attended the Christmas pantomimes at <a href="/wiki/Theatre_Royal,_Drury_Lane" title="Theatre Royal, Drury Lane">Drury Lane</a>, where he studied the art of clowning through performers like <a href="/wiki/Dan_Leno" title="Dan Leno">Dan Leno</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson71–72Chaplin47–48Weissman200982–83,_88_399-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson71–72Chaplin47–48Weissman200982–83,_88-399">[362]</a></sup> Chaplin's years with the Fred Karno company had a formative effect on him as an actor and filmmaker. Simon Louvish writes that the company was his "training ground",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish38_400-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish38-400">[363]</a></sup> and it was here that Chaplin learned to vary the pace of his comedy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson86–87_401-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson86–87-401">[364]</a></sup> The concept of mixing pathos with slapstick was learnt from Karno,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-402"><a href="#cite_note-402">[al]</a></sup> who also used elements of absurdity that became familiar in Chaplin's gags.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson86–87_401-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson86–87-401">[364]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-403"><a href="#cite_note-403">[365]</a></sup> From the film industry, Chaplin drew upon the work of the French comedian <a href="/wiki/Max_Linder" title="Max Linder">Max Linder</a>, whose films he greatly admired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynn99–100Brownlow22Louvish122_404-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynn99–100Brownlow22Louvish122-404">[366]</a></sup> In developing the Tramp costume and persona, he was likely inspired by the American vaudeville scene, where tramp characters were common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish48–49_405-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish48–49-405">[367]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Method">Method</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chaplin_Studios_postcard.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="455" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Chaplin_Studios_postcard.jpg/220px-Chaplin_Studios_postcard.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Chaplin_Studios_postcard.jpg/330px-Chaplin_Studios_postcard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Chaplin_Studios_postcard.jpg/440px-Chaplin_Studios_postcard.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chaplin_Studios_postcard.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1922 image of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin_Studios" title="Charlie Chaplin Studios">Charlie Chaplin Studios</a>, where all of Chaplin's films between 1918 and 1952 were produced</div></div></div>
<p>Chaplin never spoke more than cursorily about his filmmaking methods, claiming such a thing would be tantamount to a magician spoiling his own illusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson606_406-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson606-406">[368]</a></sup> Little was known about his working process throughout his lifetime,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrownlow7_407-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrownlow7-407">[369]</a></sup> but research from film historians – particularly the findings of <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Brownlow" title="Kevin Brownlow">Kevin Brownlow</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Gill_(film_historian)" title="David Gill (film historian)">David Gill</a> that were presented in the three-part documentary <i><a href="/wiki/Unknown_Chaplin" title="Unknown Chaplin">Unknown Chaplin</a></i> (1983) – has since revealed his unique working method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish103Robinson168_408-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish103Robinson168-408">[370]</a></sup>
</p><p>Until he began making spoken dialogue films with <i>The Great Dictator</i> (1940), Chaplin never shot from a completed script.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson173,_197,_310,_489_409-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson173,_197,_310,_489-409">[371]</a></sup> Many of his early films began with only a vague premise, for example "Charlie enters a health spa" or "Charlie works in a pawn shop".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson169_410-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson169-410">[372]</a></sup> He then had sets constructed and worked with his stock company to improvise gags and "business" using them, almost always working the ideas out on film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish103Robinson168_408-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish103Robinson168-408">[370]</a></sup> As ideas were accepted and discarded, a narrative structure would emerge, frequently requiring Chaplin to reshoot an already-completed scene that might have otherwise contradicted the story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish168Robinson166–170,_489–490Brownlow187_411-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish168Robinson166–170,_489–490Brownlow187-411">[373]</a></sup> From <i>A Woman of Paris</i> (1923) onward Chaplin began the filming process with a prepared plot,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish182_412-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish182-412">[374]</a></sup> but Robinson writes that every film up to <i>Modern Times</i> (1936) "went through many metamorphoses and permutations before the story took its final form".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson460_413-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson460-413">[375]</a></sup>
</p><p>Producing films in this manner meant Chaplin took longer to complete his pictures than almost any other filmmaker at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish228_414-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish228-414">[376]</a></sup> If he was out of ideas, he often took a break from the shoot, which could last for days, while keeping the studio ready for when inspiration returned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson234–235Cousins71_415-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson234–235Cousins71-415">[377]</a></sup> Delaying the process further was Chaplin's rigorous perfectionism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson172,_177,_235,_311,_381,_399Brownlow59,_75,_82,_92,_147_416-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson172,_177,_235,_311,_381,_399Brownlow59,_75,_82,_92,_147-416">[378]</a></sup> According to his friend <a href="/wiki/Ivor_Montagu" title="Ivor Montagu">Ivor Montagu</a>, "nothing but perfection would be right" for the filmmaker.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrownlow82_417-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrownlow82-417">[379]</a></sup> Because he personally funded his films, Chaplin was at liberty to strive for this goal and shoot as many takes as he wished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson235,_311,_223Brownlow82_418-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson235,_311,_223Brownlow82-418">[380]</a></sup> The number was often excessive, for instance 53 takes for every finished take in <i>The Kid</i> (1921).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson746Maland1989359_419-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson746Maland1989359-419">[381]</a></sup> For <i>The Immigrant</i> (1917), a 20-minute short, Chaplin shot 40,000 feet of film – enough for a feature-length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson201Brownlow192_420-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson201Brownlow192-420">[382]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1023981488" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote rquote" style="float: left; width: 33%;"><p>No other filmmaker ever so completely dominated every aspect of the work, did every job. If he could have done so, Chaplin would have played every role and (as his son Sydney humorously but perceptively observed) sewn every costume.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Chaplin biographer <a href="/wiki/David_Robinson_(film_critic)" title="David Robinson (film critic)">David Robinson</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson606_406-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson606-406">[368]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Describing his working method as "sheer perseverance to the point of madness",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish225_421-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish225-421">[383]</a></sup> Chaplin would be completely consumed by the production of a picture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrownlow157Robinson121,_469_422-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrownlow157Robinson121,_469-422">[384]</a></sup> Robinson writes that even in Chaplin's later years, his work continued "to take precedence over everything and everyone else".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson600_423-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson600-423">[385]</a></sup> The combination of story improvisation and relentless perfectionism – which resulted in days of effort and thousands of feet of film being wasted, all at enormous expense – often proved taxing for Chaplin who, in frustration, would lash out at his actors and crew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson371,_362,_469,_613Brownlow56,_136Schickel8_424-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson371,_362,_469,_613Brownlow56,_136Schickel8-424">[386]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chaplin exercised complete control over his pictures,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson606_406-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson606-406">[368]</a></sup> to the extent that he would act out the other roles for his cast, expecting them to imitate him exactly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBloom101Brownlow59,_98,_138,_154Robinson614_425-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBloom101Brownlow59,_98,_138,_154Robinson614-425">[387]</a></sup> He personally edited all of his films, trawling through the large amounts of footage to create the exact picture he wanted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson140,_235,_236_426-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson140,_235,_236-426">[388]</a></sup> As a result of his complete independence, he was identified by the film historian <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Sarris" title="Andrew Sarris">Andrew Sarris</a> as one of the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auteurism" title="Auteurism">auteur</a> filmmakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989353_427-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989353-427">[389]</a></sup> Chaplin did receive help, notably from his long-time cinematographer <a href="/wiki/Roland_Totheroh" title="Roland Totheroh">Roland Totheroh</a>, brother Sydney Chaplin, and various <a href="/wiki/Assistant_director" title="Assistant director">assistant directors</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Harry_Crocker" title="Harry Crocker">Harry Crocker</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Reisner" title="Charles Reisner">Charles Reisner</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-428"><a href="#cite_note-428">[390]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Style_and_themes">Style and themes</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><video class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="253" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="The_Kid_scenes.ogv" height="158" id="mwe_player_0" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/The_Kid_scenes.ogv/280px--The_Kid_scenes.ogv.jpg" preload="none" width="280"><source data-bandwidth="955752" data-framerate="24" data-height="480" data-shorttitle="VP9 480P" data-title="SD VP9 (480P)" data-transcodekey="480p.vp9.webm" data-width="854" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/a/ad/The_Kid_scenes.ogv/The_Kid_scenes.ogv.480p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm;
