codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Kid_scenes.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Collection of scenes from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Kid_(1921_film)" title="The Kid (1921 film)">The Kid</a></i> (1921) that demonstrate Chaplin's use of slapstick, pathos, and social commentary</div></div></div>
<p>While Chaplin's comedic style is broadly defined as <a href="/wiki/Slapstick" title="Slapstick">slapstick</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson212_429-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson212-429">[391]</a></sup> it is considered restrained and intelligent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrownlow30_430-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrownlow30-430">[392]</a></sup> with the film historian Philip Kemp describing his work as a mix of "deft, balletic physical comedy and thoughtful, situation-based gags".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKemp63_431-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKemp63-431">[393]</a></sup> Chaplin diverged from conventional slapstick by slowing the pace and exhausting each scene of its comic potential, with more focus on developing the viewer's relationship to the characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson113_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson113-80">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMast83–92_432-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMast83–92-432">[394]</a></sup> Unlike conventional slapstick comedies, Robinson states that the comic moments in Chaplin's films centre on the Tramp's attitude to the things happening to him: the humour does not come from the Tramp bumping into a tree, but from his lifting his hat to the tree in apology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson113_80-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson113-80">[71]</a></sup> Dan Kamin writes that Chaplin's "quirky mannerisms" and "serious demeanour in the midst of slapstick action" are other key aspects of his comedy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamin6–7_433-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamin6–7-433">[395]</a></sup> while the surreal transformation of objects and the employment of <a href="/wiki/In-camera_effect" title="In-camera effect">in-camera</a> trickery are also common features.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMast83–92Kamin33–34_434-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMast83–92Kamin33–34-434">[396]</a></sup> His signature style consisted of gestural idiosyncrasies like askew derby hat, drooping shoulders, deflated chest and dangling arms and tilted back pelvis to enrich the comic persona of his 'tramp' character. His shabby but neat clothing and incessant grooming behaviour along with his geometrical walk and movement gave his onscreen characters a puppet-like quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-435"><a href="#cite_note-435">[397]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chaplin's silent films typically follow the Tramp's efforts to survive in a hostile world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish60_436-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish60-436">[398]</a></sup> The character lives in poverty and is frequently treated badly, but remains kind and upbeat;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKemp63Robinson211,_352Hansmeyer4_437-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKemp63Robinson211,_352Hansmeyer4-437">[399]</a></sup> defying his social position, he strives to be seen as a gentleman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson203_438-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson203-438">[400]</a></sup> As Chaplin said in 1925, "The whole point of the Little Fellow is that no matter how down on his ass he is, no matter how well the jackals succeed in tearing him apart, he's still a man of dignity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeissman200947_439-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeissman200947-439">[401]</a></sup> The Tramp defies authority figures<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDale17_440-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDale17-440">[402]</a></sup> and "gives as good as he gets",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeissman200947_439-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeissman200947-439">[401]</a></sup> leading Robinson and Louvish to see him as a representative for the underprivileged – an "<a href="/wiki/Everyman" title="Everyman">everyman</a> turned heroic saviour".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson455,_485Louvish138_441-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson455,_485Louvish138-441">[403]</a></sup> Hansmeyer notes that several of Chaplin's films end with "the homeless and lonely Tramp [walking] optimistically<span class="nowrap"> </span>... into the sunset<span class="nowrap"> </span>... to continue his journey."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHansmeyer4_442-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHansmeyer4-442">[404]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1023981488" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote rquote" style="float: left; width: 33%;"><p>It is paradoxical that tragedy stimulates the spirit of ridicule<span class="nowrap"> </span>... ridicule, I suppose, is an attitude of defiance; we must laugh in the face of our helplessness against the forces of nature – or go insane.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Charlie Chaplin, explaining why his comedies often make fun of tragic circumstances<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson334–335_443-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson334–335-443">[405]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>The infusion of <a href="/wiki/Pathos" title="Pathos">pathos</a> is a well-known aspect of Chaplin's work,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDale9,_19,_20Louvish203_444-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDale9,_19,_20Louvish203-444">[406]</a></sup> and Larcher notes his reputation for "[inducing] laughter and tears".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELarcher75_445-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELarcher75-445">[407]</a></sup> Sentimentality in his films comes from a variety of sources, with Louvish pinpointing "personal failure, society's strictures, economic disaster, and the elements".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish204_446-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish204-446">[408]</a></sup> Chaplin sometimes drew on tragic events when creating his films, as in the case of <i>The Gold Rush</i> (1925), which was inspired by the fate of the Donner Party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson334–335_443-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson334–335-443">[405]</a></sup> Constance B. Kuriyama has identified serious underlying themes in the early comedies, such as greed (<i>The Gold Rush</i>) and loss (<i>The Kid</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuriyama31_447-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuriyama31-447">[409]</a></sup> Chaplin also touched on controversial issues: immigration (<i>The Immigrant</i>, 1917); illegitimacy (<i>The Kid</i>, 1921); and drug use (<i>Easy Street</i>, 1917).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMast83–92_432-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMast83–92-432">[394]</a></sup> He often explored these topics ironically, making comedy out of suffering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish137,_145_448-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish137,_145-448">[410]</a></sup>
</p><p>Social commentary was a feature of Chaplin's films from early in his career, as he portrayed the underdog in a sympathetic light and highlighted the difficulties of the poor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson599_449-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson599-449">[411]</a></sup> Later, as he developed a keen interest in economics and felt obliged to publicise his views,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson456_450-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson456-450">[412]</a></sup> Chaplin began incorporating overtly political messages into his films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989159_451-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989159-451">[413]</a></sup> <i>Modern Times</i> (1936) depicted factory workers in dismal conditions, <i>The Great Dictator</i> (1940) parodied <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a> and ended in a speech against nationalism, <i>Monsieur Verdoux</i> (1947) criticised war and capitalism, and <i>A King in New York</i> (1957) attacked <a href="/wiki/McCarthyism" title="McCarthyism">McCarthyism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELarcher62–89_452-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELarcher62–89-452">[414]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several of Chaplin's films incorporate autobiographical elements, and the psychologist <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a> believed that Chaplin "always plays only himself as he was in his dismal youth".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeissman1999439–445_453-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeissman1999439–445-453">[415]</a></sup> <i>The Kid</i> is thought to reflect Chaplin's childhood trauma of being sent into an orphanage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeissman1999439–445_453-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeissman1999439–445-453">[415]</a></sup> the main characters in <i>Limelight</i> (1952) contain elements from the lives of his parents,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBloom107_454-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBloom107-454">[416]</a></sup> and <i>A King in New York</i> references Chaplin's experiences of being shunned by the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson588–589_455-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson588–589-455">[417]</a></sup> Many of his sets, especially in street scenes, bear a strong similarity to Kennington, where he grew up. <a href="/wiki/Stephen_M._Weissman" title="Stephen M. Weissman">Stephen M. Weissman</a> has argued that Chaplin's problematic relationship with his mentally ill mother was often reflected in his female characters and the Tramp's desire to save them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeissman1999439–445_453-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeissman1999439–445-453">[415]</a></sup>
</p><p>Regarding the structure of Chaplin's films, the scholar <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Mast" title="Gerald Mast">Gerald Mast</a> sees them as consisting of sketches tied together by the same theme and setting, rather than having a tightly unified storyline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMast123–128_456-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMast123–128-456">[418]</a></sup> Visually, his films are simple and economic,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvish298Robinson592_457-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvish298Robinson592-457">[419]</a></sup> with scenes portrayed as if set on a stage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEpstein84–85Mast83–92Louvish185_458-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEpstein84–85Mast83–92Louvish185-458">[420]</a></sup> His approach to filming was described by the art director <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Louri%C3%A9" title="Eugène Lourié">Eugène Lourié</a>: "Chaplin did not think in 'artistic' images when he was shooting. He believed that action is the main thing. The camera is there to photograph the actors".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson565_459-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson565-459">[421]</a></sup> In his autobiography, Chaplin wrote, "Simplicity is best<span class="nowrap"> </span>... pompous effects slow up action, are boring and unpleasant<span class="nowrap"> </span>... The camera should not intrude."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaplin250_460-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaplin250-460">[422]</a></sup> This approach has prompted criticism, since the 1940s, for being "old fashioned",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrownlow91Louvish298Kamin35_461-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrownlow91Louvish298Kamin35-461">[423]</a></sup> while the film scholar Donald McCaffrey sees it as an indication that Chaplin never completely understood film as a medium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCaffrey82–95_462-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCaffrey82–95-462">[424]</a></sup> Kamin, however, comments that Chaplin's comedic talent would not be enough to remain funny on screen if he did not have an "ability to conceive and direct scenes specifically for the film medium".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamin29_463-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamin29-463">[425]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Composing">Composing</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charlie_Chaplin_playing_the_cello_1915.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="775" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Charlie_Chaplin_playing_the_cello_1915.jpg/170px-Charlie_Chaplin_playing_the_cello_1915.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Charlie_Chaplin_playing_the_cello_1915.jpg/255px-Charlie_Chaplin_playing_the_cello_1915.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Charlie_Chaplin_playing_the_cello_1915.jpg/340px-Charlie_Chaplin_playing_the_cello_1915.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charlie_Chaplin_playing_the_cello_1915.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chaplin playing the cello in 1915</div></div></div>
<p>Chaplin developed a passion for music as a child and taught himself to play the piano, violin, and cello.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson411Louvish17–18_464-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson411Louvish17–18-464">[426]</a></sup> He considered the musical accompaniment of a film to be important,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson382_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson382-205">[184]</a></sup> and from <i>A Woman of Paris</i> onwards he took an increasing interest in this area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson411_465-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson411-465">[427]</a></sup> With the advent of sound technology, Chaplin began using a synchronised orchestral soundtrack – composed by himself – for <i>City Lights</i> (1931). He thereafter composed the scores for all of his films, and from the late 1950s to his death, he scored all of his silent features and some of his short films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVance2000xiii_466-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVance2000xiii-466">[428]</a></sup>
</p><p>As Chaplin was not a trained musician, he could not read sheet music and needed the help of professional composers, such as <a href="/wiki/David_Raksin" title="David Raksin">David Raksin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Rasch" title="Raymond Rasch">Raymond Rasch</a> and Eric James, when creating his scores. Musical directors were employed to oversee the recording process, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alfred_Newman_(composer)" title="Alfred Newman (composer)">Alfred Newman</a> for <i>City Lights</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESlowik133_467-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESlowik133-467">[429]</a></sup> Although some critics have claimed that credit for his film music should be given to the composers who worked with him, Raksin – who worked with Chaplin on <i>Modern Times</i> – stressed Chaplin's creative position and active participation in the composing process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaksin_and_Berg47–50_468-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaksin_and_Berg47–50-468">[430]</a></sup> This process, which could take months, would start with Chaplin describing to the composer(s) exactly what he wanted and singing or playing tunes he had improvised on the piano.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaksin_and_Berg47–50_468-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaksin_and_Berg47–50-468">[430]</a></sup> These tunes were then developed further in a close collaboration among the composer(s) and Chaplin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaksin_and_Berg47–50_468-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaksin_and_Berg47–50-468">[430]</a></sup> According to film historian <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Vance" title="Jeffrey Vance">Jeffrey Vance</a>, "although he relied upon associates to arrange varied and complex instrumentation, the musical imperative is his, and not a note in a Chaplin musical score was placed there without his assent."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VarietyVance_469-0"><a href="#cite_note-VarietyVance-469">[431]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chaplin's compositions produced three popular songs. "<a href="/wiki/Smile_(Charlie_Chaplin_song)" title="Smile (Charlie Chaplin song)">Smile</a>", composed originally for <i>Modern Times</i> (1936) and later set to lyrics by <a href="/wiki/John_Turner_(lyricist)" title="John Turner (lyricist)">John Turner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Parsons_(lyricist)" title="Geoffrey Parsons (lyricist)">Geoffrey Parsons</a>, was a hit for <a href="/wiki/Nat_King_Cole" title="Nat King Cole">Nat King Cole</a> in 1954.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VarietyVance_469-1"><a href="#cite_note-VarietyVance-469">[431]</a></sup> For <i>Limelight</i>, Chaplin composed "Terry's Theme", which was popularised by <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Young_(broadcaster)" title="Jimmy Young (broadcaster)">Jimmy Young</a> as "<a href="/wiki/Eternally_(Charles_Chaplin_song)" title="Eternally (Charles Chaplin song)">Eternally</a>" (1952).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamin198_470-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamin198-470">[432]</a></sup> Finally, "<a href="/wiki/This_Is_My_Song_(1967_song)" title="This Is My Song (1967 song)">This Is My Song</a>", performed by <a href="/wiki/Petula_Clark" title="Petula Clark">Petula Clark</a> for <i>A Countess from Hong Kong</i> (1967), reached number one on the UK and other European charts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-471"><a href="#cite_note-471">[433]</a></sup> Chaplin also received his only competitive Oscar for his composition work, as the <i>Limelight</i> theme won an <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Original_Score" title="Academy Award for Best Original Score">Academy Award for Best Original Score</a> in 1973 following the film's re-release.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VarietyVance_469-2"><a href="#cite_note-VarietyVance-469">[431]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-473"><a href="#cite_note-473">[am]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Tramp_Essanay.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="715" data-file-width="290" decoding="async" height="419" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/The_Tramp_Essanay.jpg/170px-The_Tramp_Essanay.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/The_Tramp_Essanay.jpg/255px-The_Tramp_Essanay.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/78/The_Tramp_Essanay.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Tramp_Essanay.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chaplin as <a href="/wiki/The_Tramp" title="The Tramp">the Tramp</a>, cinema's "most universal icon", in 1915<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESarris139_474-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESarris139-474">[435]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In 1998, the film critic <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Sarris" title="Andrew Sarris">Andrew Sarris</a> called Chaplin "arguably the single most important artist produced by the cinema, certainly its most extraordinary performer and probably still its most universal icon".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESarris139_474-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESarris139-474">[435]</a></sup> He is described by the British Film Institute as "a towering figure in world culture",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFIChaplinproject_475-0"><a href="#cite_note-BFIChaplinproject-475">[436]</a></sup> and was included in <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine's list of the "<a href="/wiki/Time_100:_The_Most_Important_People_of_the_Century" title="Time 100: The Most Important People of the Century">100 Most Important People of the 20th Century</a>" for the "laughter [he brought] to millions" and because he "more or less invented global recognizability and helped turn an industry into an art".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time_100_476-0"><a href="#cite_note-time_100-476">[437]</a></sup> In 1999, the <a href="/wiki/American_Film_Institute" title="American Film Institute">American Film Institute</a> ranked Chaplin as the 10th greatest <a href="/wiki/AFI%27s_100_Years...100_Stars" title="AFI's 100 Years...100 Stars">male star</a> of <a href="/wiki/Classical_Hollywood_cinema" title="Classical Hollywood cinema">Classic Hollywood Cinema</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-477"><a href="#cite_note-477">[438]</a></sup>
</p><p>The image of the Tramp has become a part of cultural history;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHansmeyer3_478-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHansmeyer3-478">[439]</a></sup> according to Simon Louvish, the character is recognisable to people who have never seen a Chaplin film, and in places where his films are never shown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvishxvii_479-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvishxvii-479">[440]</a></sup> The critic <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Maltin" title="Leonard Maltin">Leonard Maltin</a> has written of the "unique" and "indelible" nature of the Tramp, and argued that no other comedian matched his "worldwide impact".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-480"><a href="#cite_note-480">[441]</a></sup> Praising the character, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Schickel" title="Richard Schickel">Richard Schickel</a> suggests that Chaplin's films with the Tramp contain the most "eloquent, richly comedic expressions of the human spirit" in movie history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchickel41_481-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchickel41-481">[442]</a></sup> Memorabilia connected to the character still fetches large sums in auctions: in 2006 a bowler hat and a bamboo cane that were part of the Tramp's costume were bought for $140,000 in a Los Angeles auction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-482"><a href="#cite_note-482">[443]</a></sup>
</p><p>As a filmmaker, Chaplin is considered a pioneer and one of the most influential figures of the early twentieth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECousins72Kemp8,_22Gunning41Sarris139Hansmeyer3_483-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECousins72Kemp8,_22Gunning41Sarris139Hansmeyer3-483">[444]</a></sup> He is often credited as one of the medium's first artists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchickel3–4Cousins36Robinson209–211Kaminxiv_484-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchickel3–4Cousins36Robinson209–211Kaminxiv-484">[445]</a></sup> Film historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mark_Cousins_(film_critic)" title="Mark Cousins (film critic)">Mark Cousins</a> has written that Chaplin "changed not only the imagery of cinema, but also its sociology and grammar" and claims that Chaplin was as important to the development of comedy as a genre as <a href="/wiki/D._W._Griffith" title="D. W. Griffith">D.W. Griffith</a> was to drama.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECousins70_485-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECousins70-485">[446]</a></sup> He was the first to popularise feature-length comedy and to slow down the pace of action, adding pathos and subtlety to it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchickel7,_13_486-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchickel7,_13-486">[447]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-silent_clowns_487-0"><a href="#cite_note-silent_clowns-487">[448]</a></sup> Although his work is mostly classified as slapstick, Chaplin's drama <i>A Woman of Paris</i> (1923) was a major influence on <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Lubitsch" title="Ernst Lubitsch">Ernst Lubitsch</a>'s film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Marriage_Circle" title="The Marriage Circle">The Marriage Circle</a></i> (1924) and thus played a part in the development of "sophisticated comedy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThompson398–399Robinson321Louvish185_488-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThompson398–399Robinson321Louvish185-488">[449]</a></sup> According to David Robinson, Chaplin's innovations were "rapidly assimilated to become part of the common practice of film craft".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson321_489-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson321-489">[450]</a></sup> Filmmakers who cited Chaplin as an influence include <a href="/wiki/Federico_Fellini" title="Federico Fellini">Federico Fellini</a> (who called Chaplin "a sort of <a href="/wiki/Adam" title="Adam">Adam</a>, from whom we are all descended"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson632_394-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson632-394">[357]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Tati" title="Jacques Tati">Jacques Tati</a> ("Without him I would never have made a film"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson632_394-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson632-394">[357]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Clair" title="René Clair">René Clair</a> ("He inspired practically every filmmaker"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson631_393-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson631-393">[356]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Truffaut" title="François Truffaut">François Truffaut</a> ("My religion is cinema. I believe in Charlie Chaplin…"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-490"><a href="#cite_note-490">[451]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michael_Powell" title="Michael Powell">Michael Powell</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrownlow77_491-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrownlow77-491">[452]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Billy_Wilder" title="Billy Wilder">Billy Wilder</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-story_of_film_492-0"><a href="#cite_note-story_of_film-492">[453]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vittorio_De_Sica" title="Vittorio De Sica">Vittorio De Sica</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECardullo16,_212_493-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECardullo16,_212-493">[454]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Attenborough" title="Richard Attenborough">Richard Attenborough</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-494"><a href="#cite_note-494">[455]</a></sup> Russian filmmaker <a href="/wiki/Andrei_Tarkovsky" title="Andrei Tarkovsky">Andrei Tarkovsky</a> praised Chaplin as "the only person to have gone down into cinematic history without any shadow of a doubt. The films he left behind can never grow old."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tarovsky_495-0"><a href="#cite_note-tarovsky-495">[456]</a></sup> Indian filmmaker <a href="/wiki/Satyajit_Ray" title="Satyajit Ray">Satyajit Ray</a> said about Chaplin "If there is any name which can be said to symbolize cinema—it is Charlie Chaplin… I am sure Chaplin's name will survive even if the cinema ceases to exist as a medium of artistic expression. Chaplin is truly immortal."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-496"><a href="#cite_note-496">[457]</a></sup> French auteur <a href="/wiki/Jean_Renoir" title="Jean Renoir">Jean Renoir</a>'s favourite filmmaker was Chaplin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-497"><a href="#cite_note-497">[458]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-498"><a href="#cite_note-498">[459]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hombre_disfrazado_de_Charlot_delante_del_Gran_Casino_de_San_Sebasti%C3%A1n_(1_de_2)_-_Fondo_Car-Kutxa_Fototeka.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="643" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Hombre_disfrazado_de_Charlot_delante_del_Gran_Casino_de_San_Sebasti%C3%A1n_%281_de_2%29_-_Fondo_Car-Kutxa_Fototeka.jpg/220px-Hombre_disfrazado_de_Charlot_delante_del_Gran_Casino_de_San_Sebasti%C3%A1n_%281_de_2%29_-_Fondo_Car-Kutxa_Fototeka.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Hombre_disfrazado_de_Charlot_delante_del_Gran_Casino_de_San_Sebasti%C3%A1n_%281_de_2%29_-_Fondo_Car-Kutxa_Fototeka.jpg/330px-Hombre_disfrazado_de_Charlot_delante_del_Gran_Casino_de_San_Sebasti%C3%A1n_%281_de_2%29_-_Fondo_Car-Kutxa_Fototeka.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Hombre_disfrazado_de_Charlot_delante_del_Gran_Casino_de_San_Sebasti%C3%A1n_%281_de_2%29_-_Fondo_Car-Kutxa_Fototeka.jpg/440px-Hombre_disfrazado_de_Charlot_delante_del_Gran_Casino_de_San_Sebasti%C3%A1n_%281_de_2%29_-_Fondo_Car-Kutxa_Fototeka.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hombre_disfrazado_de_Charlot_delante_del_Gran_Casino_de_San_Sebasti%C3%A1n_(1_de_2)_-_Fondo_Car-Kutxa_Fototeka.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Chaplin impersonator and his audience in <a href="/wiki/San_Sebasti%C3%A1n" title="San Sebastián">San Sebastián</a>, Spain, in 1919</div></div></div>
<p>Chaplin also strongly influenced the work of later comedians. <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Marceau" title="Marcel Marceau">Marcel Marceau</a> said he was inspired to become a mime artist after watching Chaplin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-silent_clowns_487-1"><a href="#cite_note-silent_clowns-487">[448]</a></sup> while the actor <a href="/wiki/Raj_Kapoor" title="Raj Kapoor">Raj Kapoor</a> based his screen persona on the Tramp.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-story_of_film_492-1"><a href="#cite_note-story_of_film-492">[453]</a></sup> Mark Cousins has also detected Chaplin's comedic style in the French character <a href="/wiki/Monsieur_Hulot" title="Monsieur Hulot">Monsieur Hulot</a> and the Italian character <a href="/wiki/Tot%C3%B2" title="Totò">Totò</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-story_of_film_492-2"><a href="#cite_note-story_of_film-492">[453]</a></sup> In other fields, Chaplin helped inspire the cartoon characters <a href="/wiki/Felix_the_Cat" title="Felix the Cat">Felix the Cat</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECanemaker38,_78_499-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECanemaker38,_78-499">[460]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Mickey_Mouse" title="Mickey Mouse">Mickey Mouse</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson439–444_500-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson439–444-500">[461]</a></sup> and was an influence on the <a href="/wiki/Dada" title="Dada">Dada</a> art movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimmons8–11_501-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimmons8–11-501">[462]</a></sup> As one of the founding members of United Artists, Chaplin also had a role in the development of the film industry. Gerald Mast has written that although UA never became a major company like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MGM" title="MGM">MGM</a> or <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Pictures" title="Paramount Pictures">Paramount Pictures</a>, the idea that directors could produce their own films was "years ahead of its time".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMast100_502-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMast100-502">[463]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1992, the <i><a href="/wiki/Sight_%26_Sound" title="Sight &amp; Sound">Sight &amp; Sound</a></i> Critics' Top Ten Poll ranked Chaplin at No. 5 in its list of "Top 10 Directors" of all time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-503"><a href="#cite_note-503">[464]</a></sup> In the 21st century, several of Chaplin's films are still regarded as classics and among the greatest ever made. The 2012 <i><a href="/wiki/Sight_%26_Sound" title="Sight &amp; Sound">Sight &amp; Sound</a></i> poll, which compiles "top ten" ballots from film critics and directors to determine each group's most acclaimed films,
saw <i>City Lights</i> rank among the critics' top 50, <i>Modern Times</i> inside the top 100, and <i>The Great Dictator</i> and <i>The Gold Rush</i> placed in the top 250.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-504"><a href="#cite_note-504">[465]</a></sup> The top 100 films as voted on by directors included <i>Modern Times</i> at number 22, <i>City Lights</i> at number 30, and <i>The Gold Rush</i> at number 91.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-505"><a href="#cite_note-505">[466]</a></sup> Every one of Chaplin's features received a vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-506"><a href="#cite_note-506">[467]</a></sup> Chaplin was ranked at No. 35 on <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empire_(film_magazine)" title="Empire (film magazine)">Empire</a></i> magazine's "Top 40 Greatest Directors of All-Time" list in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-507"><a href="#cite_note-507">[468]</a></sup> In 2007, the <a href="/wiki/American_Film_Institute" title="American Film Institute">American Film Institute</a> named <i>City Lights</i> the 11th <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AFI%27s_100_Years_..._100_Movies_(10th_Anniversary_Edition)" title="AFI's 100 Years ... 100 Movies (10th Anniversary Edition)">greatest American film of all time</a>, while <i>The Gold Rush</i> and <i>Modern Times</i> again ranked in the top 100.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-508"><a href="#cite_note-508">[469]</a></sup> Books about Chaplin continue to be published regularly, and he is a popular subject for media scholars and film archivists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouvishxviMaland1989xi,_359,_370_509-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouvishxviMaland1989xi,_359,_370-509">[470]</a></sup> Many of Chaplin's film have had a DVD and <a href="/wiki/Blu-ray" title="Blu-ray">Blu-ray</a> release.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-510"><a href="#cite_note-510">[471]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chaplin's legacy is managed on behalf of his children by the Chaplin office, located in Paris. The office represents Association Chaplin, founded by some of his children "to protect the name, image and moral rights" to his body of work, Roy Export SAS, which owns the copyright to most of his films made after 1918, and Bubbles Incorporated S.A., which owns the copyrights to his image and name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-511"><a href="#cite_note-511">[472]</a></sup> Their central archive is held at the archives of <a href="/wiki/Montreux" title="Montreux">Montreux</a>, Switzerland and scanned versions of its contents, including 83,630 images, 118 scripts, 976 manuscripts, 7,756 letters, and thousands of other documents, are available for research purposes at the Chaplin Research Centre at the <a href="/wiki/Cineteca_di_Bologna" title="Cineteca di Bologna">Cineteca di Bologna</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-512"><a href="#cite_note-512">[473]</a></sup> The photographic archive, which includes approximately 10,000 photographs from Chaplin's life and career, is kept at the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_de_l%27%C3%89lys%C3%A9e" title="Musée de l'Élysée">Musée de l'Elysée</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lausanne" title="Lausanne">Lausanne</a>, Switzerland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-513"><a href="#cite_note-513">[474]</a></sup> The British Film Institute has also established the Charles Chaplin Research Foundation, and the first international Charles Chaplin Conference was held in London in July 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-514"><a href="#cite_note-514">[475]</a></sup> Elements for many of Chaplin's films are held by the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Film_Archive" title="Academy Film Archive">Academy Film Archive</a> as part of the Roy Export Chaplin Collection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-515"><a href="#cite_note-515">[476]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commemoration_and_tributes">Commemoration and tributes</span></h3>
<p>Chaplin's final home, Manoir de Ban in Corsier-sur-Vevey, Switzerland, has been converted into a museum named "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaplin%27s_World" title="Chaplin's World">Chaplin's World</a>". It opened on 17 April 2016 after fifteen years of development, and is described by <a href="/wiki/Reuters" title="Reuters">Reuters</a> as "an interactive museum showcasing the life and works of Charlie Chaplin".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-516"><a href="#cite_note-516">[477]</a></sup> On the 128th anniversary of his birth, a record-setting 662 people dressed as the Tramp in an event organised by the museum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-517"><a href="#cite_note-517">[478]</a></sup> Previously, the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_the_Moving_Image_(London)" title="Museum of the Moving Image (London)">Museum of the Moving Image</a> in London held a permanent display on Chaplin, and hosted a dedicated exhibition to his life and career in 1988. The <a href="/wiki/London_Film_Museum" title="London Film Museum">London Film Museum</a> hosted an exhibition called <i>Charlie Chaplin – The Great Londoner</i>, from 2010 until 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-518"><a href="#cite_note-518">[479]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Chaplin_St_Pauls_Covent_Garden.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="615" data-file-width="814" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Charles_Chaplin_St_Pauls_Covent_Garden.jpg/190px-Charles_Chaplin_St_Pauls_Covent_Garden.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Charles_Chaplin_St_Pauls_Covent_Garden.jpg/285px-Charles_Chaplin_St_Pauls_Covent_Garden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Charles_Chaplin_St_Pauls_Covent_Garden.jpg/380px-Charles_Chaplin_St_Pauls_Covent_Garden.jpg 2x" width="190"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Chaplin_St_Pauls_Covent_Garden.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chaplin memorial plaque in <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s,_Covent_Garden" title="St Paul's, Covent Garden">St Paul's, Covent Garden</a>, London</div></div></div>
<p>In London, a statue of Chaplin as the Tramp, sculpted by <a href="/wiki/John_Doubleday_(sculptor)" title="John Doubleday (sculptor)">John Doubleday</a> and unveiled in 1981, is located in <a href="/wiki/Leicester_Square" title="Leicester Square">Leicester Square</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson677_519-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson677-519">[480]</a></sup> The city also includes a road named after him in central London, "Charlie Chaplin Walk", which is the location of the <a href="/wiki/BFI_IMAX" title="BFI IMAX">BFI IMAX</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-520"><a href="#cite_note-520">[481]</a></sup> There are nine <a href="/wiki/Blue_plaque" title="Blue plaque">blue plaques</a> memorialising Chaplin in London, Hampshire, and Yorkshire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-521"><a href="#cite_note-521">[482]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Canning_Town" title="Canning Town">Canning Town</a>, East London, the Gandhi Chaplin Memorial Garden, opened by Chaplin's granddaughter <a href="/wiki/Oona_Chaplin" title="Oona Chaplin">Oona Chaplin</a> in 2015, commemorates the meeting between Chaplin and <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mahatma Gandhi</a> at a local house in 1931.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-522"><a href="#cite_note-522">[483]</a></sup> The Swiss town of <a href="/wiki/Vevey" title="Vevey">Vevey</a> named a park in his honour in 1980 and erected a statue there in 1982.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson677_519-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson677-519">[480]</a></sup> In 2011, two large murals depicting Chaplin on two 14-storey buildings were also unveiled in Vevey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-523"><a href="#cite_note-523">[484]</a></sup> Chaplin has also been honoured by the Irish town of <a href="/wiki/Waterville,_County_Kerry" title="Waterville, County Kerry">Waterville</a>, where he spent several summers with his family in the 1960s. A statue was erected in 1998;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-524"><a href="#cite_note-524">[485]</a></sup> since 2011, the town has been host to the annual Charlie Chaplin Comedy Film Festival, which was founded to celebrate Chaplin's legacy and to showcase new comic talent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-525"><a href="#cite_note-525">[486]</a></sup>
</p><p>In other tributes, a <a href="/wiki/Minor_planet" title="Minor planet">minor planet</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3623_Chaplin" title="3623 Chaplin">3623 Chaplin</a> (discovered by Soviet astronomer <a href="/wiki/Lyudmila_Karachkina" title="Lyudmila Karachkina">Lyudmila Karachkina</a> in 1981) is named after Charlie.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchmadel305_526-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchmadel305-526">[487]</a></sup> Throughout the 1980s, the Tramp image was used by <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> to advertise their personal computers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989362–370_527-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989362–370-527">[488]</a></sup> Chaplin's 100th birthday anniversary in 1989 was marked with several events around the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-531"><a href="#cite_note-531">[an]</a></sup> and on 15 April 2011, a day before his 122nd birthday, Google celebrated him with a special <a href="/wiki/Google_Doodle" title="Google Doodle">Google Doodle</a> video on its global and other country-wide homepages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-532"><a href="#cite_note-532">[492]</a></sup> Many countries, spanning six continents, have honoured Chaplin with a postal stamp.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-533"><a href="#cite_note-533">[493]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto;overflow:hidden;width:center;max-width:808px"><div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize" style="overflow:auto"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chaplin_statues.jpg" title="Statues of Chaplin around the world, located at (left to right) 1. Trenčianske Teplice, Slovakia; 2. Chełmża, Poland; 3. Waterville, Ireland; 4. London, England; 5. Hyderabad, India; 6. Alassio, Italy; 7. Barcelona, Spain; 8. Vevey, Switzerland"><img alt="" data-file-height="230" data-file-width="824" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Chaplin_statues.jpg/800px-Chaplin_statues.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6c/Chaplin_statues.jpg 1.5x" width="800"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chaplin_statues.jpg" title="File:Chaplin statues.jpg"> </a></div>Statues of Chaplin around the world, located at (left to right) 1.<span class="nowrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Tren%C4%8Dianske_Teplice" title="Trenčianske Teplice">Trenčianske Teplice</a>, Slovakia; 2.<span class="nowrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Che%C5%82m%C5%BCa" title="Chełmża">Chełmża</a>, Poland; 3.<span class="nowrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Waterville,_County_Kerry" title="Waterville, County Kerry">Waterville</a>, Ireland; 4.<span class="nowrap"> </span>London, England; 5.<span class="nowrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Hyderabad" title="Hyderabad">Hyderabad</a>, India; 6.<span class="nowrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Alassio" title="Alassio">Alassio</a>, Italy; 7.<span class="nowrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Barcelona" title="Barcelona">Barcelona</a>, Spain; 8.<span class="nowrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Vevey" title="Vevey">Vevey</a>, Switzerland</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Characterisations">Characterisations</span></h3>
<p>Chaplin is the subject of a biographical film, <i><a href="/wiki/Chaplin_(film)" title="Chaplin (film)">Chaplin</a></i> (1992) directed by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Attenborough" title="Richard Attenborough">Richard Attenborough</a>, and starring <a href="/wiki/Robert_Downey_Jr." title="Robert Downey Jr.">Robert Downey Jr.</a> in the title role and Geraldine Chaplin playing Hannah Chaplin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-534"><a href="#cite_note-534">[494]</a></sup> He is also a character in the <a href="/wiki/Historical_drama" title="Historical drama">historical drama</a> film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cat%27s_Meow" title="The Cat's Meow">The Cat's Meow</a></i> (2001), played by <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Izzard" title="Eddie Izzard">Eddie Izzard</a>, and in the made-for-television movie <i><a href="/wiki/The_Scarlett_O%27Hara_War" title="The Scarlett O'Hara War">The Scarlett O'Hara War</a></i> (1980), played by <a href="/wiki/Clive_Revill" title="Clive Revill">Clive Revill</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-535"><a href="#cite_note-535">[495]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-536"><a href="#cite_note-536">[496]</a></sup> A television series about Chaplin's childhood, <i>Young Charlie Chaplin</i>, ran on <a href="/wiki/PBS" title="PBS">PBS</a> in 1989, and was nominated for an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emmy_Award" title="Emmy Award">Emmy Award</a> for Outstanding Children's Program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-537"><a href="#cite_note-537">[497]</a></sup> The French film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Price_of_Fame_(2014_film)" title="The Price of Fame (2014 film)">The Price of Fame</a></i> (2014) is a fictionalised account of the robbery of Chaplin's grave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-538"><a href="#cite_note-538">[498]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chaplin's life has also been the subject of several stage productions. Two musicals, <i><a href="/wiki/Little_Tramp" title="Little Tramp">Little Tramp</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Chaplin_(1993_musical)" title="Chaplin (1993 musical)">Chaplin</a></i>, were produced in the early 1990s. In 2006, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Meehan_(writer)" title="Thomas Meehan (writer)">Thomas Meehan</a> and Christopher Curtis created another musical, <i><a href="/wiki/Chaplin_(2006_musical)" title="Chaplin (2006 musical)">Limelight: The Story of Charlie Chaplin</a></i>, which was first performed at the <a href="/wiki/La_Jolla_Playhouse" title="La Jolla Playhouse">La Jolla Playhouse</a> in San Diego in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-539"><a href="#cite_note-539">[499]</a></sup> It was adapted for <a href="/wiki/Broadway_theatre" title="Broadway theatre">Broadway</a> two years later, re-titled <i>Chaplin – A Musical</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-540"><a href="#cite_note-540">[500]</a></sup> Chaplin was portrayed by Robert McClure in both productions. In 2013, two plays about Chaplin premiered in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>: <i>Chaplin</i> at the <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Theatre" title="Swedish Theatre">Svenska Teatern</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChaplinFinland_541-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChaplinFinland-541">[501]</a></sup> and <i>Kulkuri</i> (<i>The Tramp</i>) at the <a href="/wiki/Tampere_Workers%27_Theatre" title="Tampere Workers' Theatre">Tampere Workers' Theatre</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-542"><a href="#cite_note-542">[502]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chaplin has also been characterised in <a href="/wiki/Literary_fiction" title="Literary fiction">literary fiction</a>. He is the protagonist of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Coover" title="Robert Coover">Robert Coover</a>'s short story "Charlie in the House of Rue" (1980; reprinted in Coover's 1987 collection <i>A Night at the Movies</i>), and of <a href="/wiki/Glen_David_Gold" title="Glen David Gold">Glen David Gold</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Sunnyside_(novel)" title="Sunnyside (novel)">Sunnyside</a></i> (2009), a historical novel set in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">First World War</a> period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-543"><a href="#cite_note-543">[503]</a></sup> A day in Chaplin's life in 1909 is dramatised in the chapter titled "Modern Times" in <a href="/wiki/Alan_Moore" title="Alan Moore">Alan Moore</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Jerusalem_(Moore_novel)" title="Jerusalem (Moore novel)">Jerusalem</a></i> (2016), a novel set in the author's home town of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northampton,_England" title="Northampton, England">Northampton</a>, England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-544"><a href="#cite_note-544">[504]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chaplin was brought to life in a <a href="/wiki/Comic_strip" title="Comic strip">comic strip</a> <a href="/wiki/Celebrity_comics" title="Celebrity comics">bearing his name</a> that ran for 30 years in the British pre-<a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">war</a> humorous comic <i><a href="/wiki/Funny_Wonder" title="Funny Wonder">Funny Wonder</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-comicskingdom.com_545-0"><a href="#cite_note-comicskingdom.com-545">[505]</a></sup> Begun in 1915, the strip was drawn primarily by Bertie Brown;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stringer_546-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stringer-546">[506]</a></sup> it was one of the earliest comic strips inspired by the popularity of a celebrity. A similar strip, <i><a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin%27s_Comic_Capers" title="Charlie Chaplin's Comic Capers">Charlie Chaplin's Comic Capers</a></i>, by Stuart Carothers <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-547"><a href="#cite_note-547">[507]</a></sup> and later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elzie_C._Segar" title="Elzie C. Segar">Elzie C. Segar</a>, was syndicated in the United States from March 29, 1915, until September 16, 1917.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holtz_548-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holtz-548">[508]</a></sup> In France in 1922, Raoul Thomen created the comic strip <i>Les Aventures Acrobatiques de Charlot</i> ("Charlot's Acrobatic Adventures").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomen_549-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomen-549">[509]</a></sup> Thomen's strip ran in French children's magazines<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomen_549-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thomen-549">[509]</a></sup> for nearly 20 years. Charlot's comic strip adventures were continued by other artists, lasting until 1963. The strip was collected in many albums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-URen_550-0"><a href="#cite_note-URen-550">[510]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_recognition">Awards and recognition</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charlie_Chaplin_walk_of_fame.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1404" data-file-width="1560" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Charlie_Chaplin_walk_of_fame.jpg/170px-Charlie_Chaplin_walk_of_fame.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Charlie_Chaplin_walk_of_fame.jpg/255px-Charlie_Chaplin_walk_of_fame.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Charlie_Chaplin_walk_of_fame.jpg/340px-Charlie_Chaplin_walk_of_fame.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charlie_Chaplin_walk_of_fame.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Star on the <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_Walk_of_Fame" title="Hollywood Walk of Fame">Hollywood Walk of Fame</a> at 6755 Hollywood Boulevard.</div></div></div>
<p>Chaplin received many awards and honours, especially later in life. In the <a href="/wiki/1975_New_Year_Honours" title="1975 New Year Honours">1975 New Year Honours</a>, he was appointed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Knight_Commander_of_the_Order_of_the_British_Empire" title="Knight Commander of the Order of the British Empire">Knight Commander of the Order of the British Empire</a> (KBE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-551"><a href="#cite_note-551">[511]</a></sup> He was also awarded honorary <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Letters" title="Doctor of Letters">Doctor of Letters</a> degrees by the University of Oxford and the University of Durham in 1962.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson598–599_362-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson598–599-362">[327]</a></sup> In 1965, he and <a href="/wiki/Ingmar_Bergman" title="Ingmar Bergman">Ingmar Bergman</a> were joint winners of the <a href="/wiki/Erasmus_Prize" title="Erasmus Prize">Erasmus Prize</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson610_552-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson610-552">[512]</a></sup> and, in 1971, he was appointed a Commander of the National Order of the Legion of Honour by the French government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-553"><a href="#cite_note-553">[513]</a></sup>
</p><p>From the film industry, Chaplin received a special <a href="/wiki/Golden_Lion" title="Golden Lion">Golden Lion</a> at the Venice Film Festival in 1972,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson625–626_554-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson625–626-554">[514]</a></sup> and a Lifetime Achievement Award from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Film_Society_of_Lincoln_Center" title="Film Society of Lincoln Center">Lincoln Center Film Society</a> the same year. The latter has since been presented annually to filmmakers as The Chaplin Award.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-555"><a href="#cite_note-555">[515]</a></sup> Chaplin was given a star on the <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_Walk_of_Fame" title="Hollywood Walk of Fame">Hollywood Walk of Fame</a> in 1972, having been previously excluded because of his political beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams311_556-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams311-556">[516]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chaplin received three <a href="/wiki/Academy_Awards" title="Academy Awards">Academy Awards</a>: an <a href="/wiki/Academy_Honorary_Award" title="Academy Honorary Award">Honorary Award</a> for "versatility and genius in acting, writing, directing, and producing <i>The Circus</i>" in <a href="/wiki/1st_Academy_Awards" title="1st Academy Awards">1929</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-circus_206-1"><a href="#cite_note-circus-206">[185]</a></sup> a second Honorary Award for "the incalculable effect he has had in making motion pictures the art form of this century" in <a href="/wiki/44th_Academy_Awards" title="44th Academy Awards">1972</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson623–625_379-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson623–625-379">[344]</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Original_Score" title="Academy Award for Best Original Score">Best Score</a> award in <a href="/wiki/45th_Academy_Awards" title="45th Academy Awards">1973</a> for <i><a href="/wiki/Limelight_(1952_film)" title="Limelight (1952 film)">Limelight</a></i> (shared with Ray Rasch and Larry Russell).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VarietyVance_469-3"><a href="#cite_note-VarietyVance-469">[431]</a></sup> He was further nominated in the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Actor" title="Academy Award for Best Actor">Best Actor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Original_Screenplay" title="Academy Award for Best Original Screenplay">Best Original Screenplay</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Picture" title="Academy Award for Best Picture">Best Picture</a> (as producer) categories for <i>The Great Dictator</i>, and received another Best Original Screenplay nomination for <i>Monsieur Verdoux</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-557"><a href="#cite_note-557">[517]</a></sup> In 1976, Chaplin was made a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BAFTA_Academy_Fellowship_Award" title="BAFTA Academy Fellowship Award">Fellow</a> of the <a href="/wiki/British_Academy_of_Film_and_Television_Arts" title="British Academy of Film and Television Arts">British Academy of Film and Television Arts</a> (BAFTA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-558"><a href="#cite_note-558">[518]</a></sup>
</p><p>Six of Chaplin's films have been selected for preservation in the <a href="/wiki/National_Film_Registry" title="National Film Registry">National Film Registry</a> by the United States <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a>: <i>The Immigrant</i> (1917), <i>The Kid</i> (1921), <i>The Gold Rush</i> (1925), <i>City Lights</i> (1931), <i>Modern Times</i> (1936), and <i>The Great Dictator</i> (1940).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-559"><a href="#cite_note-559">[519]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Filmography">Filmography</span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">An <a href="/wiki/MI5" title="MI5">MI5</a> investigation in 1952 was unable to find any record of Chaplin's birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Chaplin biographer David Robinson notes that it is not surprising that his parents failed to register the birth: "It was easy enough, particularly for music hall artists, constantly moving (if they were lucky) from one town to another, to put off and eventually forget this kind of formality; at that time the penalties were not strict or efficiently enforced."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson10_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson10-5">[5]</a></sup> In 2011 a letter sent to Chaplin in the 1970s came to light which claimed that he had been born in a Gypsy caravan at <a href="/wiki/Black_Patch_Park" title="Black Patch Park">Black Patch Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/Smethwick" title="Smethwick">Smethwick</a>, Staffordshire (part of Birmingham at the time). Chaplin's son <a href="/wiki/Michael_Chaplin_(actor)" title="Michael Chaplin (actor)">Michael</a> has suggested that the information must have been significant to his father for him to retain the letter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Regarding the date of his birth, Chaplin believed it to be 16 April, but an announcement in the edition of 11 May 1889 of <i>The Magnet</i> stated it as the 15th.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinsonxxiv_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinsonxxiv-8">[8]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sydney was born when Hannah Chaplin was 19. The identity of his biological father is not known for sure, but Hannah claimed it was a Mr. Hawkes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson3_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson3-11">[10]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Hannah became ill in May 1896, and was admitted to hospital. Southwark Council ruled that it was necessary to send the children to a workhouse "owing to the absence of their father and the destitution and illness of their mother".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson19_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson19-20">[18]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to Chaplin, Hannah had been booed off stage and the manager chose him – as he was standing in the wings – to go on as her replacement. He remembered confidently entertaining the crowd, and receiving laughter and applause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson17Chaplin18_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson17Chaplin18-33">[30]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-38">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Eight Lancashire Lads were still touring until 1908; the exact time Chaplin left the group is unverified, but based on research, A. J. Marriot believes it was in December 1900.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarriot213_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarriot213-37">[33]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">William Gillette co-wrote the <i>Sherlock Holmes</i> play with <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Conan_Doyle" title="Arthur Conan Doyle">Arthur Conan Doyle</a>, and had been starring in it since its New York opening in 1899. He had come to London in 1905 to appear in a new play, <i>Clarice</i>. Its reception was poor, and Gillette decided to add an "after-piece" called <i>The Painful Predicament of Sherlock Holmes</i>. This short play was what Chaplin originally came to London to appear in. After three nights, Gillette chose to close <i>Clarice</i> and replace it with <i>Sherlock Holmes</i>. Chaplin had so pleased Gillette with his performance in <i>The Painful Predicament</i> that he was kept on as Billy for the full play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson59–60_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson59–60-46">[41]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-55">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Chaplin attempted to be a "Jewish comedian", but the act was poorly received and he performed it only once.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson68Marriot81–84_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson68Marriot81–84-54">[48]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-70"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-70">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">$4,100 in 2021 dollars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-US_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-69">[62]</a></sup></span>

<li id="cite_note-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-79">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Robinson notes that this was not strictly true: "The character was to take a year or more to evolve its full dimensions and even then – which was its particular strength – it would evolve during the whole rest of his career."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson114_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson114-78">[70]</a></sup></span>

<li id="cite_note-111"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-111">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">After leaving Essanay, Chaplin found himself engaged in a legal battle with the company that lasted until 1922. It began when Essanay extended his last film for them, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burlesque_on_Carmen" title="Burlesque on Carmen">Burlesque on Carmen</a></i>, from a two-reeler to a feature film (by adding out-takes and new scenes with <a href="/wiki/Leo_White" title="Leo White">Leo White</a>) without his consent. Chaplin applied for an injunction to prevent its distribution, but the case was dismissed in court. In a counter-claim, Essanay alleged that Chaplin had broken his contract by not producing the agreed number of films and sued him for $500,000 in damages. In addition, the company compiled another film, <i><a href="/wiki/Triple_Trouble_(1918_film)" title="Triple Trouble (1918 film)">Triple Trouble</a></i> (1918), from various unused Chaplin scenes and new material shot by White.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson149–152_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson149–152-110">[98]</a></sup></span>

<li id="cite_note-196"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-196">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In her memoirs, Lita Grey later claimed that many of her complaints were "cleverly, shockingly enlarged upon or distorted" by her lawyers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland198996_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland198996-195">[176]</a></sup></span>

<li id="cite_note-227"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-227">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Chaplin left the United States on 31 January 1931, and returned on 10 June 1932.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson664–666_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson664–666-226">[204]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-257"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-257">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Chaplin later said that if he had known the extent of the Nazi Party's actions he would not have made the film; "Had I known the actual horrors of the German concentration camps, I could not have made <i>The Great Dictator</i>; I could not have made fun of the homicidal insanity of the Nazis."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaplin388_252-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaplin388-252">[229]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-260"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-260">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Speculation about Chaplin's racial origin existed from the earliest days of his fame, and it was often reported that he was a Jew. Research has uncovered no evidence of this, and when a reporter asked in 1915 if it was true, Chaplin responded, "I have not that good fortune." The Nazi Party believed that he was Jewish and banned <i>The Gold Rush</i> on this basis. Chaplin responded by playing a Jew in <i>The Great Dictator</i> and announced, "I did this film for the Jews of the world."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson154–155_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson154–155-259">[235]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-272"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-272">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In December 1942, Barry broke into Chaplin's home with a handgun and threatened suicide while holding him at gunpoint. This lasted until the next morning, when Chaplin was able to get the gun from her. Barry broke into Chaplin's home a second time later that month, and he had her arrested. She was then prosecuted for <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/vagrant" title="wikt:vagrant">vagrancy</a> in January 1943 – Barry had been unable to pay her hotel bills, and was found wandering the streets of Beverly Hills after taking an overdose of <a href="/wiki/Barbiturate" title="Barbiturate">barbiturates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989198–201_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989198–201-271">[246]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-276"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-276">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to the prosecutor, Chaplin had violated the act when he paid for Barry's trip to New York in October 1942, when he was also visiting the city. Both Chaplin and Barry agreed that they had met there briefly, and according to Barry, they had sexual intercourse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989204–205_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989204–205-274">[248]</a></sup> Chaplin claimed that the last time he was intimate with Barry was May 1942.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson523–524_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson523–524-275">[249]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-284"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-284">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Carol Ann's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_group" title="Blood group">blood group</a> was B, Barry's was A, and Chaplin's was O. In California at this time, blood tests were not accepted as evidence in legal trials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989205–206_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989205–206-283">[256]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-289"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-289">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Chaplin and O'Neill met on 30 October 1942 and married on 16 June 1943 in <a href="/wiki/Carpinteria,_California" title="Carpinteria, California">Carpinteria, California</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaplin423–444Robinson670_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaplin423–444Robinson670-287">[259]</a></sup> Eugene O'Neill disowned his daughter as a result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheaffer623,_658_288-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheaffer623,_658-288">[260]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-296"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-296">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">equivalent to $83,000 in 2021</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-313"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-313">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Chaplin had already attracted the attention of the FBI long before the 1940s, the first mention of him in their files being from 1922. J. Edgar Hoover first requested that a Security Index Card be filed for Chaplin in September 1946, but the Los Angeles office was slow to react and only began active investigation the next spring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989265–266_311-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989265–266-311">[281]</a></sup> The FBI also requested and received help from <a href="/wiki/MI5" title="MI5">MI5</a>, particularly on investigating the false claims that Chaplin had not been born in England but in France or Eastern Europe, and that his real name was Israel Thornstein. MI5 found no evidence of Chaplin being involved in the Communist Party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[282]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-322"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-322">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In November 1947, Chaplin asked <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Picasso" title="Pablo Picasso">Pablo Picasso</a> to hold a demonstration outside the US embassy in Paris to protest the deportation proceedings of Hanns Eisler, and in December, he took part in a petition asking for the deportation process to be dropped. In 1948, Chaplin supported the unsuccessful presidential campaign of <a href="/wiki/Henry_A._Wallace" title="Henry A. Wallace">Henry Wallace</a>; and in 1949 he supported two peace conferences and signed a petition protesting the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peekskill_Riots" title="Peekskill Riots">Peekskill incident</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaland1989256–257_321-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaland1989256–257-321">[290]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-327"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-327">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Limelight</i> was conceived as a novel, which Chaplin wrote but never intended for publication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson549–570_326-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson549–570-326">[294]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-341"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-341">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Before leaving America, Chaplin had ensured that Oona had access to his assets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson580_340-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson580-340">[307]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-345"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-345">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Robinson speculates that Switzerland was probably chosen because it "was likely to be the most advantageous from a financial point of view".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson581_344-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson581-344">[310]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-386"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-386">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The honour had already been proposed in 1931 and 1956, but was vetoed after a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Office" title="Foreign and Commonwealth Office">Foreign Office</a> report raised concerns over Chaplin's political views and private life. They feared the act would damage the reputation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_honours_system" title="British honours system">British honours system</a> and relations with the United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-385"><a href="#cite_note-385">[350]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-392"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-392">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Despite asking for an Anglican funeral, Chaplin appeared to be agnostic. In his autobiography he wrote, "I am not religious in the dogmatic sense<span class="nowrap"> </span>... I neither believe nor disbelieve in anything<span class="nowrap"> </span>... My faith is in the unknown, in all that we do not understand by reason; I believe that<span class="nowrap"> </span>... in the realm of the unknown there is an infinite power for good."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChaplin287_391-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChaplin287-391">[355]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-402"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-402">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Stan_Laurel" title="Stan Laurel">Stan Laurel</a>, Chaplin's co-performer at the company, remembered that Karno's sketches regularly inserted "a bit of sentiment right in the middle of a funny music hall turn".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson86–87_401-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson86–87-401">[364]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-473"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-473">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although the film had originally been released in 1952, it did not play for one week in Los Angeles because of its boycott, and thus did not meet the criterion for nomination until it was re-released in 1972.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-472"><a href="#cite_note-472">[434]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-531"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-531">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">On his birthday, 16 April, <i>City Lights</i> was screened at a gala at the <a href="/wiki/Dominion_Theatre" title="Dominion Theatre">Dominion Theatre</a> in London, the site of its British premiere in 1931.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-528"><a href="#cite_note-528">[489]</a></sup> In Hollywood, a screening of a restored version of <i>How to Make Movies</i> was held at his former studio, and in Japan, he was honoured with a musical tribute. Retrospectives of his work were presented that year at <a href="/wiki/BFI_Southbank" title="BFI Southbank">The National Film Theatre</a> in London,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewSundayTimes_529-0"><a href="#cite_note-NewSundayTimes-529">[490]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Munich_Stadtmuseum" title="Munich Stadtmuseum">Munich Stadtmuseum</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewSundayTimes_529-1"><a href="#cite_note-NewSundayTimes-529">[490]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Modern_Art" title="Museum of Modern Art">Museum of Modern Art</a> in New York, which also dedicated a gallery exhibition, <i>Chaplin: A Centennial Celebration</i>, to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-530"><a href="#cite_note-530">[491]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div>
